REPUBLIC OF KENYA
MICRO- AND SMALL ENTERPRISE TAINIG AND TECHNOLOGY PROJECT
CREDIT AND PROJECT SU1MMRY
Borrower:           Republic of Kenya
lBenefciaries:      The main beneficiaries are micro- and small enterprises/entrepreneurs,
maay of whom are in the informal sector and/or are poor urban
workers
Execul  Agencies:  Ministry of Researcl, Technical Training and Technology (MRTT&T)
Jua Kali Federation and Associations
Amomlt:             SDR 15.7 million (US$ 21.83 million)
Terms:              Standard, with 40 years maturity
O)nlendI  Tenns:   Not Applicable
Project Objectives:   The long term aims of the project are to enhance entrepreneurial
development in the private sector and, more specifically, reduce
constraints to employment promotion and income enhancement in the
micro- and small enterprise (informal) sector in Kenya. The specific
project objectives are to: (a) provide skill upgrading for about 60,000
Jua Kali personnel (mainly in the manufacturing sector); and diversify
and improve the quality of about 200 public and private sector training
providers; (b) increase the access of small scale entrepreneurs to
appropriate technology and marketing information and attendant
infrastructure (sites and services) on a pilot basis; and (c) improve the
policy and institutional environment, particularly the strategic policy
analysis, monitoring and evaluation functions of the MRTT&T and the
management capacity of the Jua Kali Federation and Associations.
lProject Description:  The project would accomplish the above objectives through a set of
policies for enabling the entrepreneurial environment and developing a
national training strategy to improve the quality, access, and
sustainable financing of relevant skill upgrading and technology for the
informal sector, and a related investment program. The investment
program includes: (a) a micro- and small enterprise training fund; (b)
a technology information, innovation and research program and pilot
infrastructure development; and (c) institutional development. The
Project would make provision for increasing women's representation
in manufacturing through skills training and technology development
suitable for upgrading their enterprises or bringing them into the
sector. MRTT&T would use private sector contractors, training and
research institutions, the Jua Kali Federaion and Associations and



iii
employers as the primary agents to promote and implement the
project.
Benefits:           Expected benefits are: (a) improved skills and an expanded informal
sector -- including female-owned manufacturing enterprises -- with
increased employment opportunities and earnings through product
quality enhanccment and diversification, secure worksite and improved
infrastructure, better marketing and increased access to technology
choice; (b) a downsized and more focused Ministry of Research,
Technical Training, and Technology capable of policy analysis, setting
training standards, facilitating technical and managerial training and
related technology information and innovation; (c) the progressive
empowerment of the Jua Kali sector which would enhance its
participation in the economic development process; and (d) gradual
inclusion (graduation) of the informal sector into the modem sector by
establishing a legal and contractual basis for its unfettered
development.
Risks:              The possible risks relatte to: (a) the ability of the Government to
specify and implement training and private seetor development policies
to enable informal sector entrepreneurs to operate more effectively;
(b) administrative and over-sight capacity of MRTT&T and Jua Kali
Federation/Associations, including decision-making about Training
Fund allocation and management; (c) beneficiary response exemplified
by their willingness to avail vouchers and to recover user costs for
training, technology information and infrastructure; and (d) the
capacity to sustain policy and project interventions. To overcome
these risks, the project would be conditioned on a policy letter and
action plan ensuring key enabling policies for the Jua Kali sector.
Training Fund replenishments would be allomated on an annual basis,
once .greed and acceptable levels of performance in administration,
award aliocation, and utilization criteria are met.
The process for awarding competitive individual skill upgrading
vouchers, contract training grants and applied technology study tours
and research grants would be evaluated in an annual project review.
A mid-term project evaluation would be included to allow for project
redirection, if necessary. Since institutional development is a major
component focusing on the Jua Kali Federation/Associations and the
MRTT&T, as well as management and planning skills of
entrepreneurs, one significant outcome would be the capacity to
sustain policy and project interventions. With the inclusion of partial
cost recovery through training fees and a modernized Training Levy,
the financial sustainability of training investments would also be
strengthened. In addition, cost recovery measures through the
infrastructure revolving fund managed by the Associations with
oversight from the MRTT&T should contribute to the sustainability of
physical investments.

REPUBLIC OF KENYA
MICRO- AND SMALL ENTERIRISE TRAINIG AND TECHNOLOGY PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
A. Macroeconomy and Employmnet
lhe Kenyan Economy and Employment Challenge
1.1    The Kenyan economy now faces a formidable employment challenge as a result of
slow economic growth combined with the rapid expansion of population. During the first two
decades after Independence in 1963, the economy grew at a rate of nearly seven (7) percent,
which was better than the performance of most developed and developing countries. The
economic growth rate, however, was not sustained in the 1980s, and more recently declined
to 2.2 percent in 1991. The poor economic performance in recent years is attributed mainly
to policy failures - erosion of fiscal and macroeconomic discipline, protective import
substitution oriented policy, excessive and inappropriate forms of Government intervention in
the private sector, etc. - rather than structural impediments or lack of resources. Meanwhile,
the population of 24 million is growing at about 3.4 percent, down from 3.6 percent in the
recent past, but still among the highest growth rates in the world. The total labor force is
expected to grow at an average rate of about 4.1 percent per annum between 1991 to 2000
and over 6 percent in urban areas, accelerated by rural-urban migration and rising labor force
participation, especially of women. I/
1.2     There is no doubt that if present trends contimne, the economy wil be unable to
absorb 492,000 new entrants per year to the labor market with a considerable baclog of
unemployed and underemployed workers. 4/ Given the increased educational attainment
and prevailing attitudes about jobs, most new entrants would be aspiring to employment
outside smallholder agriculture. S/ Total employment outside smallholder agriculture, i.e.
employment in the modem and informal sectors, in 1991 was 2 miUlion. If this employment
were to grow at its trend growth rate of 5.2 percent between 1984 and 1991, then the size of
new job creation would average about 128,000 a year between 1992 to 2000. This implies
that only about a quarter of new entrants would be absorbed by these sectors. Moreover, if
all new entrants were to be employed only in the modem wage sector, then employment in
that sector has to grow at an average rate of 17 percent p.a. during the same period. An
average growth rate of employment in this sector was 3.6 percent between 1981 to 1991.
1.3   It is clearly not possible for aU the entrants to be employed in the modem sector.
Given that the informal sector has a sponge effect in employment, it may be concluded that a
large part of the labor force will inevitably end up faUing within the informal sector. For
example, if their present trend growth rates continue, the modem and the small farm sectors
together will generate 240,000 jobs on average p-.r year. This would leave the balance of
250,000 jobs per year to be created primarily in the rural non-farm and urban micro- and
3/    World Bank, Emolovmenm Growth for Povert, Allevto,n. CEM, green cover (199). 11650-KE).
4/    Today, the unemployment in uthan ateas is about 23 perem, whie in rural a  prvale underemployment is a
more serous problem dmn open unemploymen.
5/1    Almost all new entrants to the labor force would have completed a primny educadon of eight years, and about a third
would have completed secondary school or beyond.



2
small enterprise (informal) sector, of which 75% or 190,000 would be in the urban areas6/.
The challenge becomes not only to stimulate employ-inent in this sector - since this will
happen inevitably - but also to create the conditions for graduation into the formal sector and
ensure quality jobs which are sustainable.
1.4     The pressure of popu!ation growth on a sluggish economy has deteriorated the
standard of living in Kenya. In urban areas, for example, unemployment increased to 23
percent in 1991 from 16 percent in 1986 and the average real wage today is only 45 percent
of its level in 1980. Considering the persistent high dependency ratio of 119, which is the
highest in the world, this may imnply a larger proportion of the population lives at or below
the poverty line.7/ Although the land tenure system in rural areas enabled the smallholder
farmning sector to absorb most people without raising open unemployment, the pressure of a
growing population on increasingly scarce fertile land has led to increased landlessness and to
the farming of environmentally fragile land. Without a change in agricultural technology and
significantly increased productivity, poverty will become widespread in rural areas. In fact,
more than 20 percent of rural households in 1992 could not afford a nutritionally adequate
diet and over one million children under five are stunted due to undernourishment over an
extended period. Overall, if the present trend continues, Kenya's per capita income of $340
per year will double only after 40 years. At that point, it would reach a level which would
place it slightly ahead of average incomes already achieved by Senegal and Zimbabwe, and
slightly behind the level of Cote d'Ivoire.
1.5     To reduce unemployment and poverty, rapid growth of the economy is the most
urgent need. At the same time, adequate policy to harness population growth should be given
a high priority for a long term effect. Given the elasticity of employment to product.on
registered by the Kenyan economy in recent years, a very high growth rate of GDP would be
required in order to absorb the additional labor force. For exam,ple, if the trend elasticities
were to remain the same in the modern and informal sectors, then the combined GDP in these
sectors should grow at an average rate of 13 percent to absorb all new entrants. This
discussion leads to the conclusion that Kenya must raise not only the growth rate of GDP but
also the elasticity of job creation with respect to GDP growth. Therefore, a labor intensive
growth path should be adopted. To achieve this goal, one strategy is to promote a private
sector development which targets larger labor absorption. Another strategy is to develop the
informal sector which has already higher employment-GDP elasticity: 1.1 versus .85 in the
informal and modern sector, respectively. These two strategies should be pursued
simultaneously, particularly facilitating the transition of the infonnal sector into the formal
sector. Aware of these, the Kenyan Government is now pursuing a comprehensive strategy for
the enhancement of the private sector as a whole, including the informal sector.
6/      Note that the 1993 CEM projected that the urban inforn=l sector would absorb new labor market entrants of 79,000,
at a target growth rate of 12 percent p.a. for employment in this sector. Since the CEM aimed at developing a strategy for
private formal sector development, the sponge effect of the informal sector was not considered. The possible increase in rural-
urban migration was also assumed to remait at the present level.
2/      Dependency ratio is defined as the atio of ckilJren and retirement-age adults to the number of working age adults. 15-
64 years old. A high dependency ratio means dtat a relatively smaller proporion of the population is engaged in economic
activities, which thus has an adverse impact on the welfare of households.



3
Development Stratgy and Medirin-term Framework
1.6   Sessional Paper No.t of 1986, Economic Maga=  for Renewed Growth
articulates the Government's long-term development strategy. It forms the basis of the
Development Plan for 1989-93 and the series of Policy Framework Papers (PFP) including
the current one for 1991/92-1993/94. The Sessional Paper emphasizes the need to create
productive employment opportunities for a labor force that in the year 2000 is expected to be
90 percent greater than the level in 1985. Accordingly, it sets a high target GDP growth rate,
which is to be generated primarily by the private sector. Increasingly, the Government's role
is expected to be geared mcre to the provision of an appropriate enabling environment than its
direct interventionist role in economic activity. The strategy, whichs is basically sound,
emphasizes the need to increase agricultural productivity, strengthen links between rural non-
farm activities and the formal sector, and restructure manufacturing to improve efficiency and
enhance export competitiveness. In addition, the informal sector is especially highlighted as a
primary vehicle for social and economic development.
1.7    As spelled out in the current PFP, the Government's specific aims for 1991/92-
1993/94 are to attain output growth of over 5%; reduca- inflation to the average (effective)
level forecast for Kenya's main trading partners (about 5%) by 1993; and reduce the external
current account deficit to a sustainable level of 4% of GDP by 1993/94. To achieve these
objectives, the Govermnent intend. to: (a) reduce the budget deficit to levels that can be
sustained by foreign and non-inflationry domestic financing, and that do not crowd out the
private sector; (b) design and implement a program to restructure and privatize the state
corporation sector; (c) continue the progress in implementing the import liberalization
program and removing the anti-export bias in the system: (d) remove disincentiveL to
production and investment through elimination of the General Prce Control Order as well as
other market regulations; and (e) continue progress in implementing the financial sector
liberalization program, including the removal of the ceiling on bank lending rates, and the
development of secondary markets in debt instruments and capital markets.
B. The Bank's Country Asdsi e Progrm
1.8   There are three priority areas for the Bank's involvement: (a) assistin pic sector
adjustment, involving the divestture and restructuring of parastatals, the down-sizing and re-
organizadon of the civil service, and the rationalization of the remaining public expenditures;
(b) developing a grivate secor through reform of the legal and regulatory environment and
the financial sector, and the elimination of the remaning foreign exchange, price, marketing
and production controls; and (c) taeted intervention to alleviate poverlv, including
improvements in the delivery of social services to low-income groups, with special attention
to the development needs of women and residents in arid and semi-arid areas. I/
1.9    Given the informal sector's strong potential for emnloyment creation, the best way to
support this is to hmprove the productivity of existing enterprises so that they can hire more
workers and create stable jobs. If employment in the informal sector is to grow at a high
rate, the enhancement of productivity and scale at the Jua Kali enterprise level will be
necessary. Development of the informal sector will also lead to poverty reduction through
A/    Coumnty Suatw Paper. Febuy, 1993.



4
increasing the employment opportunities and incomes among the poor and marginal segment
of the society. This combined with the emergence of a middle class will contribute to an
improvement of income inequality. The proposed project would provide support to the
Kenyan Government toward meeting these goals.

II. INFORMAL SECTOR GROWTH AND TRAINING
A. Overview of the Informal Sector
2.1     The 1972 International Labor Office (ILO) study on Kenya used the term  "informal
sector" to describe a set of activities which had been neglected in previous economic analysis
and policies. The informal sector, however, is an ambiguous and comprehensive term
embracing everything from small-scale manufacturing and repair to trade, transport,
construction, and services. This heterogeneity raises a serious definitional question. While
almost every research, study and project adopt their own definition, the criteria which have
been commonly used to distinguish the infr.,: -it sector include: (a) whether enterprises
comply with government regulations, e.g., business registration and minimum wages, etc.,
and whether they pay taxes; (b) whether they are involved in illegal or illegitimate activities
such as prostitution or crime; (c) size of enterprises; (d) amount of assets; and (e) amount of
energy use. WVhile criterion (a), in particular, is consistent with the objectives of the proposed
project, it is not helpful to characterize the informal sector as one evading regulations and
taxes, since many small enterprises have a variety of registrations and pay some taxes such as
custom duties on inputs. This makes it difficult to define beneficiaries of this project. Hence,
it is more useful to identify the informal sector on the basis of size of establishment, measured
by number of employees. In this respect, a working definition of the informal sector is
adopted to cover micro (zero to nine employees) and small enterprises (10-49 employees) to
serve as a criterion to determine the beneficiaries. 2/ Further, the term "Jua Kali", literally
meaning "hot sun" in Kiswahili, is used colloquially to refer to enterprises specializing in the
manufacture of products and providing productive services, rather than traders per se, who
are the majority of informal sector entrepreneurs.
2.2     Almost every survey conducted in Kenya adopts a different definition and the size of
the sector varies depending on the definition. The definition used in the Small-Scale
Enterprises (SSE) Survey includes "self employed persons in open markets, in market stalls,
in undeveloped lots, or on street pavements within urban centers". 10/ Under this
definition, businesses that are household-based or have more permanent premises and employ
extra workers, which are more likely to have growth potential, are for the most part excluded.
The 1993 CEM estimated that by adding 20 percent adjustment, the 1991 informal
employment was 598,513 at 350,066 establishments with one to twenty persons. 1I/ The
informal sector constitutes 98 percent of all businesses in the country and contributes 37
percent of total urban employment, about 1.6 million in 1991. Urban centers accounted for
9/      This defintion does not exclude smal firms in the modem sector in order to maxinize the potential for employment
generation development through sub-contracting as well as cost recovery of investments. However, there would be provisions to
ensure that project benefits would not be captured only by the better off enterprises. See eligibility criteria for infrastrucure,
voucher program, and contract training scheme in Annexes 8 and 9.
10/     The Small-Scale Enterprises Survey (Infomal Sector Survey before 1987) is anmually conducted by the Central Bureau
of Statistics (CBS). It covers all 91 towns of 2,000 persons or more, and adopts a systematic, street-by-street count of informal
(open-air) activities. This method makes it difficult to achieve full coverage of the sector on a nadonal scale and therefore,
under-representation may be likely.
11      For example, a recent survey conducted in Kibera, suburban area of Nairobi, with an estimated population of 25,000
found that 35 percent of al households had small enterprises operatig from their residences. Such firms mostly hired I to 20
employees. These enterprises would not be included in CBS data. (Patker. oan and Aleke-Dondo, C. 'Kiberas SmaU Scale
Enterprise Sector: Baseline Survey Report' Febsuaiy 1991). For a more detailed atgument, see CEM 1993.



6
nearly two thirds of total employment, while Nairobi makes up 25 percent of all employment
in the sector. About 60% of informal operators are involved in trade with the remainder
divided equally between manufacturing and other services.
2.3    The informal sector recently has made a substantial contribution to the expansion of
employment in Kenya. According to CBS data, employment in the informal sector grew at 14
percent per annum in the last three years as compared to 2.3 percent in the modern wage
sector, steadily rising from 9.5 percent in 1985 (Annex 1, Table 1.3). This means that more
than three quarters of new jobs outside smallholder agriculture are created in the informal
sector. The manufacturing sub-sector, in particular, showed higher employment growth (19
percent). There is also a large geographical variation in the growth rate, e.g. the highest,
16.5 percent for Nairobi and the lowest, 6.9 percent for Western Province. This is attributed
to a disparity in market opportunities, that is, the demand for goods and services.
2.4    There are three types of workers in the informal sector: working owners, paid
workers, and unpaid family workers. Most enterprises employ fewer than five workers in
addition to the owner. The overall level of employment per establishment is on average about
1.7 workers including the owner. This means that each establishment hires less than one
employee in addition to an owner. A majority of the total workers in the informal sector are
unpaid family workers (53 percent). While females constitute about one third of the non-
owner workers in the informal sector they work mostly as unpaid family workers. Only one-
quarter of the non-owner women are paid workers in comparison to two third of hired men.
The manufacturing sector, in general, hires more workers than the other sectors. The share
of paid workers is also the largest in the manufacturing sector with nearly 42 percent of total
paid workers. Especially, enterprises specializing in wood and wood products or in
fabricating metals generate the largest employment, while those in retail trade, clothing and
leather industries generate almost no extra employment besides the owner.
2.5    Women constitute about 49 percent of Kenya's informal entrepreneurs in urban areas,
exceeding their 40 percent share of the total urban labor force. Kenya, however, is consistent
with global patterns whereby women are more likely to be involved in trade and services than
in manufacturing. Even in manufacturing women are concentrated in leather and clothing
industries. The Kenyan Government estimates that women constitute 60 to 70 percent of
those in trade, about half of those are engaged in services, and less than 10 percent in the
manufacturing sector. This means that female owned establishments have a lower
employment generation effect since female-dominant industries have the least number of hired
employees (para. 2.4). The proportion of women entrepreneurs is higher in regions with high
concentration of Kikuyus. This may be attributed to the fact that Kikuyu women have
developed income-generating self-help groups that provide a training ground for business
activity.
2.6    While there is scarce information on the wages and income of the informal sector, the
few available statistics confirm the common perception that the informal sector workers
comprise the lower income group. For example, the 1991 estimates in Nairobi indicate that
the Jua Kalis' average monthly wage of Ksh 740 is substantially below the minimum monthly
wage of Ksh 964. A recently published Economic Survey (1993) shows that an average
income of informal sector worker headed households is lower than fonnal sectr.: worker's
households: Ksh 3,420 versus Ksh 3,749 in Nairobi and Ksh 2,284 versus Ksh 3,498 in
Eastern Province. However, earnings from some informal sector activities are generally
comparable and sometimes higher than the income from the paid employees in the modern



7
sector. Garages, metal fabrication, and wood processing give the highest eamings to owners
and highest wages paid to workers, while tailoring and shoentaking the lowest. Tailoring and
shoemaking are those trades which have been most exposed to competition from urban-based,
mass-produced goods. Overall, employees receive about one-half of the mean eamings of
owners.
2.7   Initial capital invested in informal sector enterprises mostly comes from personal and
family resources. The owner's personal savings account for about 70 percent and loans from
friends and relatives for 18 percent of the investment in micro and small enterprises. The
relative importance of personal savings are the same for all businesses, regardless of the
owners' gender, the business location and type of activity. Only one to two percent of small
and micro-entrepreneurs are reported to have used institutional sources of capital. Many
informal non-trading enterprises are established by those who were originally employed in
formal sector occupations, usually in small Asian firms, where significant transfers of skills to
Kenyan-African enterprises occurred.
2.8    While the low capital requirements and high labor intensity of the informal sector
makes it a promising area for absorbing employment, the high turnover of microenterprises
comprising the majority of informal sector enterprises also make the jobs precarious. In 1990
informal sector enterprises had an average life of three years: about two thirds of all Kenyan
enterprises with less than five employees perish within three years and only about 15% of
enterprises last longer than five years. The life of enterprises was longest in manufacturing,
especially metal fabrication, garages, and construction, and shortest in commerce. This seems
to reflect the difficulties of entering these activities because of the relatively high capital and
skill barriers. The first four years of operation are critical for survival to obtain market
competitiveness, management capacity and skill acquisition. 12/
2.9   Informal sector businesses cater to the rural and urban lower income groups
(consumer goods and services, mostly with low income elasticity). The informal sector
manufacturing firms are also an important support system for the modern sector by providing
domestic inputs and services used by medium and large-scale firms, besides being customers
for many goods produced in the modem sector. These two sectors are expected to have
closer linkages in the future through sub-contracting. Therefore, the growth of such firms is
tied closely to increases in employment and income in other parts of the economy.
B. Constaints to Informal Sector Growth and Development
2.10   The recent Bank Private Sector Development Strategy (PSDS) report found that the
private sector, as a whole, suffers from the government's policy failures -lack of
macroeconomic discipline, poor and uneven enforcement of laws and excessive regulations.
The informal sector shares these obstacles, and is further constrained by: lack of worksite
security and basic infrastructure; limited access to formal credit; low skill levels, and inability
to acquire information on marketing and technology opportunities. These factors are again
mutually reinforcing. For example, insecurity of worksites restricts access to credit, leads to
12/    House, W. J., 0. K. Ddara, and D. Mccormiick, 1993. Urban Self-Employment in Kenya. Panacea or Viable
Strategy? World Develonmeu, Vol. 21, No. 7. pp 1205-1223.



8
local authorities' harassment. and creates disincentives for developing and investing in the
worksites. therelw hampering improvements in technology and productivity.
Policy Enviroimient
2.11   T'`  maini constraint to growth and emnployment is the disabling policy environment.
Although the Goverinment has undertaken various actions to remove the constraints hampering
market efficiency, powitciial informal sector entrepreneurs ma)y still face various institutional
barriers to entry. handicaps to survival and growvth, and impedimcnts to transition from the
informal to the formal sector.
2.12   Enterprises in Kenva are required both to register and obtain various licenses. These
requirements may inhibit starting and expanding informal sector enterprises because they
involve complicated processes and high monetary and transaction costs. Under the
Registration of Business Names Act, the registration process involves two stages: first,
compan) registration with the Registrar of Companies in the Office of the Attorney General,
and second, a series of mandatory national registrations with various line ministries. Without
registration, licenses cannot be obtained thereby blocking access to formal credit. Company
registration is automatic but may be cumbersome for informal sector entrepreneurs since it
can only be carried out in Nairobi. While the registration fee is trivial for small-scale
enterprises, it can cost up to four percent share value to larger limited liability companies,
therefore, being a detriment to the growth of such enterprises in the long run. The second
stage requires five separate registrations from four ministries: manufacturers registration and
Bureau of Standards (Ministry of Industry), National Social Security Fund, Occupational
Health (Ministry of Labor), and the Central Bureau of Statistics. While registration
applications are rarely denied, the procedures are complicated and frequent delays are
reported, leading to large transaction costs for quick completion. A Management Information
System (MIS) has been recently introduced in the Attorney General's Office to expedite the
process. The procedure should be consolidated, simplified, and decentralized in order to
allow for registration outside Nairobi.
2.13   There are three main classes of licenses affecting the informal sector: national licenses
required under the Trade Licensing Act (TLA), those under other special Acts, e.g. health
and tourism, etc., and the licenses with local authorities. National licenses are issued
according to the type of business activity: Trade License for manufacturing, General License
for wholesaling, a Retailers License for retail distribution, etc.. Licenses are also issued for
specific localities and product/service lines and any changes in these characteristics require
official revision. Each license is valid onlv for one year and its renewal requires the same
procedures as starting new firms. The license fees vary by activity and location but are not
gradated by the size of the firm, discriminating against small-scale firmis. In 1992, Trade
License fees are Ksh 6,600 in Nairobi and Ksh 3,300 elsewhere. For most informal sector
enterprises these are too high to be paid annually. In addition to national licenses, there is a
range of licenses required by the local authorities. There is no statutory requirement for
harmonization across local authorities in the number of licenses or the level of fees. Since
national level licenses are issued in a manner similar to national licenses, the adverse effect of
local licenses is ntore serious on the informal than the formal sector for the same reasons.
Overall, obtaining a license, in principal, should not be difficult nor too expensive, but, in
practice, it is cumbersome and costly. There are no explicit criteria for refusing to grant a
license and no formal mechanism for appeal, leaving wide scope for harassment and rent
seeking in the licensing process. The Trade Licensing Act should be amended to streamline



9
the registration processes through consolidation of licenses, establishment of a 'one stop
service" for acquisition of licenses, and elimination of the current annual renewal
requirement.
2.14   Another majc- institutional barrier hampering entry to and growth of the informal
sector is found to be access to land for enterprises, in particular land entitlement. Absence of
legal titles contributes to lack of access to credit and to local harassment, and creates
disincentives for developing and investing in the worksites, which thereby prevents
improvements in technology and productivity. In Kenya there are three basic types of land
ownership: government land, trust land, and private land. In urban areas, most of the land is
leased to private individuals or companies by either the government or the responsible local
authority. The government in the recent past allocated almost all the land suitable for housing
and commercial/industrial purposes and accordingly, undeveloped or unallocated public land
that could be available to industry or the informal sector is scarce. Many plots have been
allocated to politically powerful individuals at below mnarket prices who use them for
speculative purposes. Most of these plots have not been developed by the allotees violating
legal requirement to develop within a certain period of time (msostly two years) since the
allotment. The Government therefore would have to facilitate and ensure accessing urban
land to informal sector entrepreneurs (Jua Kalis). Lack of basic infrastructure -access roads,
water and drainage, and electricity in the sites- acts as a barrier to growth of the infonmal
sector, adversely affecting the transformation in technology, e.g. shifting from hand to power
tools. While informal sector entrepreneurs are willing to pay user fees for electricity, running
water, etc., the costs of the initial connection for these services are too great for most
individuals. Consumers are also discouraged to purchase Jua Kali goods due to the
difficulties to reach the sites caused by the absence of access roads and footpaths.
2.15   Although Kenya has a well-developed and flexible system of labor relations,
=relations on redundancy and waae gwidelines may act as a disincentive for the informal
sector to graduate into the formal sector. The redundancy provision constrains firms' flexible
adjustment to economic changes by restricting their autonomy in hiring and firing employees.
While large formal sector firms cite the restrictions on permanent redundancy as their main
problem with the labor laws, informal sector entrepreneurs in general do not comply with
these laws. The need to comply therefore makes it costly for the informal sector to graduate.
It also encourages the formal sector to adopt more capital-intensive technology and thereby
lowering demand for labor in the long-run. Another impediment to achieving a flexible labor
market is wage guidelines, which are currently designed to increase employment by keeping
labor costs down. The guidelines distort the relationship between productivity and pay by: (a)
stipulating that the percentage of wage increase should decrease at higher levels of pay; and
(b) limiting increases in wages for any single industry if such increases would force wages up
in other firms or industries less able to afford them. The first stipulation leads to wage
compression, thereby reducing incentives for workers to obtain more skills. The second
stipulation, which restricts pay disparity across a similar industry or firm, also impedes labor
mobility by limiting the ability of better perfonning firms to offer better pay to attract
workers from other firms. Overall, wage guidelines may lead to underinvestment in training
and thereby lower productivity of labor. 13/ The avoidance of these regulations gives the
informal sector more autonomy, providing it an incentive for remaining informal.
131    For the detailed artalysis, see Chapter 4. CBM, 1993.



1o
Amendment of the redundancy regulation and the remnoval of wage guidelines would improve
efficiency in the labor market and facilitate graduation of the informal sector.
2.16   In addition to these constraints to entty and growth of the informal sector, there are
various other legal, .egulatory barriers which specifically affect the operation of the informal
sector only. For instance, the Vagrancv Act. Chiefs' Authoritv and Public Nuisance Acts are
outdated laws enacted in colonial times to control the Africans. These laws justify local
authorities' harassment of the informal sector and therefore hamper the growth of the sector.
There are a range of laws regulating building standards such as the Local Government
Adoptive Bv-Laws (Building) Order and Public Health Act. While these laws serve different
purposes, they set up building standards unrealistic for Jua Kalis, who are thereby subject to
harassment. Additional outdated or inappropriate laws which impede the sector are the
Electric Power Act. Shop Hours Act. Mombasa Shop Hours Act, etc.. To promote the
sector's development, these laws should be amended.
2.17 In the past, absence of an appropriate Watent system had a negative effect on
technology advancement. The Kenyan patent law provided only for registration of patents
already granted in the United Kingdom. Any Kenyan invention, therefore, had to be first
patented in the United Kingdom. However, many technological innovations which are
relevant to the informal sector and need protection in Kenya could not be patented in the
United Kingdom. Failure to protect patent rights dampened local inventors' incentives. For
example, a research study shows that Kenya Ceramic Jiko (KCJ) (cooking stove), which is a
technological adaptation rather than an innovation, could not be patented in the UK and
neither il Kenya. Lack of patent protection deterred investors initially interested in
commercializing the stove and some of its variants.L4-/
2.18 To solve these problems, the Government enacted the Industrial Property Act in
December, 1989, which revised the patent system more relevant to the Jua Kali sector by
amending the grant and regulations of patents, utility models, and industrial designs. Utility
model means any form, configuration or disposition of some appliance, utensil, tool,
electrical, or electronic circuitry, instume, handicraft mechanism or any part of the same in
order to allow a better functioning or use of the utility. The introduction of utility models, a
type of "petty patents", thus can provide an incentive for the promotion of inventive and
innovative activity and facilitates the acquisition of tec)nology. The information on these
changes, however, has not been effectively disseminated, remaning unknown to the Jua Kali
sector and would require the sensitization using jua kali newsletter or other mass media.
2.19   Access to credit is a major obstacle to the growth of the informal sector. It is
recognized that an increased flow of funds to micro and small scale enterprises will only be
achieved if financing is carried out on a self-sustainable basis. This financing would be
provided at commercial rates of interest, and through domestic funds mobilization and without
subsidies either to the beneficiaries or the providers of such financing. This requires a major
reform in the financial sector, without which partal assistance would not help the informal
sector to obtain accessibility to credit. The proposed project cannot support a segmented
approach to financial intermediation. The project would, however, promote credit

accessibility through provision of infrastructure, which can be used as collateral, and
encourage access to information about credit extension services in general. 151
Training Supply and Skllls Demand
2.20   Supply Side. For the first time since 1988, technical and vocational training are the
primary responsibility of a single ministry: the Ministry of Technical Training and Applied
Technology (MTTAT) which, moreover, is charged with the development of programs
benefitting the informal sector. The creation of the MTTAT and the explicit inclusion of the
Jua Kali or productive informal sector in its portfolio must be recognized as a major step in
increasing the relevance of formal training (The organization and functions of the renamed
Ministry (MRTT&T) are described in Annex 7). However, although public training
institutions provide the largest proportion of technical training in Kenya, 16/ none were
designed specifically to train for employment or self-employment in the informal sector; and
even though some public training institutions are underutilized, they are not equipped nor
sufficiently flexible to provide skills upgrading (technical and managerial) for a changing
workforce.
2.21    The MRTT&T, with donor assistance, has begun to develop Jua Kali related programs
to a_vance indigenous and informal technology and promote private initiatives (see section I
E). Many of these efforts have not been part of a coherent training strategy. Projects have
often been designed and implemented in piecemeal fashion. Central Government funding of
skills training has been very limited, and the potential for appropriations-in-aid needs to be
developed. Institutions like the Industrial Training Centers (ITCs) lack financial autonomy to
reinvest the income they earn from fees and products they sell, thus there is no real incentive
to offer training services in a competitive environment. All told, the VET training share in
total central Government expenditure represents only about 2% of the education budget in
1992 and 2.4% in 1993 respectively. Most of this share goes to the National Polytechnics
and Institutes of Technology, which are not so relevant to the informal sector in contrast to
the lower level institutions (YPs) and which are more likely to produce Jua Kalis (see Annex
6 for enrollment and expenditure statistics in the VET sector.)
2.22   Apart from the informal sector's own entry level apprentice training, very little sldll
upgrading is available for informal sector clients.l7/ The few NGOs that offer technical
training programs have a very limnited outreach. However, NGO's currently account for as
much as 85 percent of the management training offered to the small business sector. A
private sector that provides management and small business trang has only begun to

2.23   The ITCs, however, which train apprentices sponsored by industry under the
Industrial Training Levy system, have most recently opened their doors to the Jua Kali
artisans for evening courses which are both fee paying and subsidized by the Levy. Annual
enrollment for evening courses (estimated at more than 2,000) is higher than for regular
courses (estimated at less than 500). The Jua Kali demand for evening courses at the ITCs,
as well as trade testing, is probably because trade tests are ideal for registering and grading
the practical qualifications of the Jua Kalis. They are also the stepping stone towards
employment in the formal sector. However, the ITCs are primarily oriented to the large and
medium scale industries and the trade test system does not adequately reflect the skills and
technologies found in the informal sector.
2.24   In spite of these developments, the Industrial Training Levy system excludes the
informal sector to a great extent (although Jua Kali trainees increasingly take industrial trade
tests). The proposed project would support a comprehensive study of the Industrial Training
Levy system to improve its access, efficiency and effectiveness. Consequently, the Industrial
Training Act is likely to be amended based on the study's findings. Proposals for extending
the Training Levy system to include informal sector enterprises would be considered at that
time.
2.25   Sklfls Demand. Lack of managerial and entrepreneurial skills and appropriate
technology are invariably cited as major constraints to the competitiveness and growth of
informal sector enterprises. 19/ Skills upgrading and on-the-job training is not readily
available and the vast majority of entrepreneurs seem to rely on informal training (see Table
2, Annex 6 for educational background of Jua Kali entrepreneurs). While infornal training
has its own merits and benefits, the lack of systematic skills development, basic concepts and
theory, and associated technology, is seen as its biggest weakness which may result in
bottlenecks preventing the development of informal sector enterprises.
2.26   One core problem of Kenyan private sector enterprises is the low and declining
productivity. Average labor productivity stagnated during the 1980s, probably as a result of
the poor development of skills among the workforce and the decline in investment in
machinery and equipment. Even in manufacturing, where real investment actually rose in
Kenya, productivity gains during much of the 1980s have been insignificant in comparison to
those averaged by developed and other developing countries.2Q/
2.27   Managerial competence in informal sector enterprises is low. An estimated 4% of
informal sector entrepreneurs' have a commercial education (see Annex 6, Table 2). There is
a need for management skills to organize production lines, handle raw material inventories
and maintain quality control in order to market new products or locally produced items that
are competitive with imported products. Marketing assistance is also required to help create

new linkages that would broaden marketing options among producers or between providers
and distributors through tenders, sub-contracts, co-operative arrangements or trade
associations.
2.28   The low level of technical skills is another constraint to the growth of the micro- and
small enterprise sector 21/. The most important deficiency is an absence of design and
development skills needed for new products or for modifying existing products. This is
caused partly by lack of technical skill upgrading as well as the low educational levels of Jua
Kali members, which limit their capacity to adapt to skill requirements and new technologies
in a market economy (see Annex 6, Table 2). Moreover, public technical training institutions
are geared to formal, theoretical training and seldom do technical needs assessments of the
informal sector. Thus, courses are not market oriented; and the poor development of
technical skills and management is a potential bottleneck in re-orienting the production
structure towards the dynamic external market. 22/
2.29   In view of limited education and poor literacy, numeracy and problem solving skills,
the informal sector entreprer.eurs and operators generally require flexible and diverse training
programs that are tailored to their particular needs and constraints. Female entrepreneurs,
especially, confront special constraints with respect to training. These include mobility and
time constraints problems: when training programs are centralized, women may not be able or
permitted to leave their families and concomitant responsibilities because of their dual roles or
their obligations as heads of households. Women may be further constrained by lack of
resources and time which places them at a disadvantage in meeting the opportunity costs of
training, such as transportation, time lost, etc.. Finally, women often have lower literacy and
educational levels than men which requires more basic skill training for them.
Technology Development
2.30   Technology -- which consists of equipment, tools, products, processes, materials, and
skills - is a critical factor in stimulating sustained growth in employment and incomes. It can
facilitate innovation in product design, diversification and quality improvement, thus enriching
maarket opportunities. In Kenya, lack of access to appropriate (i.e., labor intensive) and new
technology, as well as limited markets for informal sector products, are key constraints to
survival and growth of micro- and small businesses. Competition in the informal sector is
often intense, particularly when many entrepreneurs produce and sell the same product.
Marketing problems experienced by Jua Kali entrepreneurs are due especially to limited
access to new technology. Jua Kali operators tend to be unfamiliar with new technologies or
have no way of gauging the appropriateness of different technologies. Moreover, beside
management contracts and technology embodied in imported machinery and equipment, little
use is made of the rapidly evolving technology available at low cost in the international
market. There is no service now available to help domestic firms in identifying technological
choices and conducting assessments. 23/
i'    PSDS, pp.96-97, op.cit.
j/     P3DS, p. 97, on.itL
23/    PSDS, p. 29, oR.cit



14
2.31   Constraints to innovation and dissemination of technology in the sector are also linked
to an almost total unavailability of capital and the application of very simple or rudimentary
technology in small production units. Othe- key constraints are the low education levels and
the lack of access of microentrepreneurs to information about available technologies that may
be appropriate for their needs. Besides technological training to improve basic production
skills, micro- and small enterprises must develop a greater capacity for further innovation.
One way is to organize demonstration projects by the public and private sector. The
technological heterogeneity in the small informal sector production units requires different
types of support from specialized institutions. Training on its own will not solve the labor
factor in productive units, which is increasingly interrelated to technological problems as well
as production management and organization ones. Therefore, skills development should be
integrated within a larger context to be more effective and realistic.
2.32   Many existing micro- and small enterprises have obsolete technology and will need
wholesale modernization of their production technologies to compete in the market. This
requires identification of technologies suitable for microenterprises and for increasing the
quality of their products. Experience elsewhere shows that many of the most effective
technological changes for micro-and small enterprises have arisen from the adaptation of
traditionally used tools, equipment, or production methods, rather than the introduction of
newer technologies developed in industrialized countries. Appropriate technologies should
meet the producers' needs and priorities and be cornpatible with labor intensive development
(CEM, 1993), as well as cultural attitudes and infrastructural support, by using the skills and
resources available to them which are also typically less capital and more labor intensive.
2.33   To create opportunities for growth, i.e., new arrivals in the sector and income, the
issue of limited product diversity must be addressed. It can be done through market research,
product diversification, and improvement in quality, design, and marketing. While there may
be little certainty about which new productive areas will emerge, potential areas can be
identified once a mechanism, by which training and technology needs can be rnonitored, is
established under the project. To ensure the use of new technologies, research and
development institutions must involve entrepreneurs themselves throughout the entire process
of research, development and technology dissemination. Technological assistance can help
these enterprises improve the organization and management of their production lines.
2.34   At present, a variety of institutions, notably the National Polytechnics and Kenya
Industrial Research Development Institute (KIRDI), engage in training and research to help
overcome the constraints of manufacturers in adopting, operating, and maintaining new
technologies. To this end, they provide extension services, demonstrations, and follow-up
technical assistance. However, this type of assistance is generally not accessible to micro-
and small business enterprises. Only the Center for Research and Technology (CRT)
provides limited services to Jua Kali artisans.
Institutional Capacity
2.35   As a relatively new ministry, MRTT&T's technical capacity is weak, especially in
terms of its ability to coordinate efforts to tackle the issues mentioned above and the various
challenges which lie ahead, e.g., policy analysis, setting training standards, and facilitating
technical and managerial skill upgrading. While at the central and provincial levels almost all
authorized staff positions have been filled, at district level less than half of the authorized
positions have been filled. This means that the MRTT&T does not yet have a fully effective



15
means of coordinating and monitoring technical training .t the local level. Although there are
a large number of training institutions in the country, their coordination and control is still
below expectations. This problem is aggravated by the lack of consistent and up-to-date
official statistics on numbers of trainers, trainees and even training institutions themselves.
This situation encourages the duplication of effort as well as the underutilization of training
capacity.
2.36   Another issue which remains to be tackled, in the light of the civil service reform and
associated downsizing of the staff, is the definition of functions and duties and the related staff
redeployment and development of the MRTT&T and its regional offices. Further, the
potential overlap between other ministries, paticularly Labour (Manpower Development and
Employment), needs to be addressed. The project would finance an organizational study of
the MRTT&T which would contribute to laying the groundwork for staff redeployment and
development in the context of civil service reform. The Jua Kali Federation/Associations,
which are only at the begimhiing of their institutionalization process, face similar organizational
tasks. The number of registered and well organized, less politicized Jua Kali groups is still
limited but expected to increase substantially under the proposed project in an effort to
develop the private sector and Jua Kali Associations, as well as beneficiary participation. (See
Annex 5 for a detailed review of the Jua Kali Federation/Associations and Annex 4 which
shows the organizational ch.arts of MRTT&T and Jua Kali Associations).
2.37   Finally, the MRTT&T lacks monitoring and evaluation capacity to maintain a
responsive and competitive training system. It lacks information on the types of training
offered in the various institutions, their performance and quality of programs. To obtain such
information would require a management information capacity, in addition to training
program evaluation. While informal sector apprenticeships tend to be demand-driven, surveys
as well as feedback systems are needed to monitor the demand for specific skill upgrading. A
monitoring and management information system would also be useful in terms of providing
guidance and criteria for funding specific training interventions under the proposed project.
This would require greater collaboration with the formal and informa! sector industries and
enterprises, as well as the MRTT&T, the National Industrial Training Council (NITC) and the
National Jua Kali Federation/Associations, not only in determining market oriented training
policy and programs, but also in developing and maintaining standards in skills upgrading and
associated trade testing.
C. The Role of the Public Sector in Informal Sector Development
2.38   In order to realize the potential for employment creation in the informal sector, an
enabling environment for business has to be provided so that the productivity of micro- and
small enterprises can be enhanced. In this process the government assumes an important role
by (a) removing obstacles identified in section II B, and (b) formulating and implementing
appropriate policies for the informal sector as a part of a commitment to private sector
development. Yet, underinvestment in human capital and technology development - caused
in part by insufficient information, the inability of small enterprises to provide training,
economies of scale in training, etc.- may not raise produc-tivity levels to their full potential.
Public sector intervention is required and justified in these areas to correct market failures.
2.39   Training. Informal sector operators tend to receive employer-based skill training
below the social optimum for a number of possible reasons. First, since they cannot afford



16
the absence of wages or lower wages during training, conside. aig their already low wages,
most of the training costs must be borne by employers. 24/ Workers, however, are mobile
and cannot be bound to an enterprise. Thus, if they leave, the benefits from workers' training
cannot be fully captured by the enterprises when high labor turnover and poaching (recruiting
trained workers from other enterprises) exist. This externality is a disincentive for employers
to invest in training. Even when workers are willing to pay for skills training, they find it
difficult to finance training because they lack access to credit in the capital market. Workers
are also likely to have limited information on the availability and quality of training. Second,
informal sector firms may not have a sufficient number of employees requiring the same type
of skills training, or they may lack the technology and equipment required for on-the-job
training. This leads to high overhead costs in organizing training courses and significant
economies of scale, hence underinvestment in training. Informal sector enterprises, because
they produce the same goods, are not likely to cooperate in organizing training since they may
view each other as competitors. Third, informal sector employers are involved in day-to-day
management of their enterprises and therefore have neither the skills nor the time to organize
training for themselves or for trainees. Finally, informal sector enterprises may not even be
aware of their skill needs or the technological alternatives available to them.
2.40   These factors lead to market failure in informal sector training. The public sector
should therefore intervene to equate the social rate of return of such training to its social cost.
The above points show that both social and private rates of return as well as social and private
costs differ: private costs and benefits are those borne by individual workers and enterprises.
To make up for this difference betvreen private and social net benefit, interventive measures
such as subsidy to trainees or technical assistance are indicated. Moreover, if the trarsaction
and information costs associated with training can be lowered by public interventions which
exploit economies of scale, efficiency gains can be realized by measures such as the
organization of training for group of informnal sector workers.
2.41    Technology. Applied research to develop and adapt better quality appropriate for
informal sector entrepreneurs is one of the very few exaumples for pure public goods. 25/
Micro- and small scale entrepreneurs can be innovative, but they do not have the capacity to
conduct their own research and development or to test new technology. Once a technology
has been developed by someone, however, they can easily adopt or copy the technology at
low cost. This "free rider" issue acts as a disincentive against the private sector's
development of technologies to be sold to informal sector enterprises. Public sector
intervention may facilitate the realization of large potential benefits derived from R&D
activities. The public sector can play a catalytic role in upgrading technologies. Coordination
would be required with universities and science and technology institutes. In addition to the
development of new appropriate technology, information on existing technology available
domestically and abroad should be disseminated through exhibitions, trade fairs and study
tours.
24/     Note that a worker's total training costs include the opportunity costs, i.e., value of time of training time, as well as
direct and indirect costs.
/       Pure public goods are characterized by zero marginal costs of provision to an additional user once they have been
produced and high exclusion costs of preventing people from using the gooc- without paying for them. The latter characteristic
is known as the 'free-rider problem'.



17
2.42   Infrastructure. The public sector has an important role to play in the provision of
secure worksites and basic infrastructure for informal sector enterprises. While these
enterprises are willing to pay user fees for running water, electricity, telephone, etc. the
connection costs for these services are too high for some individuals. The public sector can
generate economy of scale for the connection of utilities and the construction of sheds by
organizing groups of informal enterprises. Insecurity of the worksites also discourage
investing in infrastructure. Once public sector intervention reduces the costs associated with
investing in infrastructure and uncertainty through creating an enabling environment, informal
sector entrepreneurs themselves or private sector investors will be willing to invest in
infrastructure for the informal sector. Thus, in the future public sector intervention may be
reduced to the provision of techni_al assistance and supervision.
2.43   Provision of training and infrastructure for informal enterprises can also respond to
equity concerns. Considering that the informal sector constitutes the poor segment of the
society, public interventions can be justified by boosting its income, thus allowing the
effective demand for basic human needs to increase.
2.44 Institutional Capacity. Absence of updated data and statistics on the activities and
needs of the informal sector, training institutions, their quality, etc. contribute to
inefficiencies. While the potential benefits derived from the generation and dissemination of
information are large, the market may fail to provide socially optimal levels of information
since the benefits do not accrue to the private sector, at least in the initial stage. In addition,
absence of standards and uniform criteria for trade testing and certification discourages
workers' mobility. Overall, the dissemination of information on job availability and
characteristics, and workers' quality (through the improvement of trade tests) will ease job
search time and facilitate the job matching process and thus reduce these costs for both
employees and employers.
2.45   In principle, cost recovery should be possible, but should not push the participants'
demiand for services below the socially optimal level. Public sector intervention does not
mean Government intervention. Such services can be contracted out or delivered through
NGOs and private sector providers. The government can still play a facilitating and catalytic
role through policy-making, implementing and supervision. Since the public sector should be
responsive to the needs of the informal sector, beneficiary involvement is essential.
D. Government Sectoral Policies and Objectives
2.46   The Government recognizes the importance of the informal sector in employment
creation, income distribution, and rural-urban balance as spelled out in several key sectoral
policy papers. Sessional Paper No. 1 of 1986, Economic Management for Renewed Growth
highlighted the informal and small-scale enterprises as a primary vehicle for social and
economic development. These sentiments are reflected in the Sixth National Development
Plan. 1989-93, the first to give prominence to the informal sector. Redressing the neglect of
the informal sector in past economic planning, it is proposed that the Government act as a
catalyst and a facilitator to promote the informal sector, leaving a major role for the private
sector. Further emphasis on the importance of informal sector enterprises was given by the
Presidential Committee on Employment in the report, Development and Emplovment in
Kenya: A Strategy for the Transformation of the Economy (the "Ndegwa Report"). This
discusses, inter alia, the strategic performance of the informal sector as a source of income



18
and employment for the majority of labor market entrants between now and the year 2000.
Based on these discussions, Sessional Paper No. 2 of 1992, Small Enterprise and Jua Kali
Development in Kenya sets a comprehensive policy framework which would enhance: (a)
direct assistance to individual entrepreneurs and small scale enterprises; (b) the transition of
micro and small scale enterprises into medium size enterprises; (c} access to credit for the
informal sector; (d) access to information on market and appropriate technologies; and, (e) an
enabling policy environment through redressing licensing, tax, security of worksite and other
regulatory restrictions on informal sector activities.
2.47   The 1992 Sessional Paper foresees a major role for MRTT&T in the development of
the informal enterprise sector. It would develop and implement innovative skill upgrading
programs promoting self-employment and entrepreneurship as well as rural business centers
for information dissemination, research and product development. To some extent, Small
Business Centers (SBC) have been established and attached to the training institutions and Jua
Kali Associations and other donor sponsored entrepreneurship education projects. Finally,
since 1993, MRTT&T's mission includes Jua Kali mobilization, informal sector marketing
and publicity, applied technology, research and development services. However, in order to
develop the full potential of the sector more rapidly, actions are required to implement
policies in the areas of training and technology, the regulatory framework and finance. This
would be done on the basis of greater private sector involvement and cost sharing.
2.48 The proposed project would be an important long term investment in developing both
industrial technology and technical/managerial training in Kenya. Good Government policies
and strong private sector support institutions are the basic premise of the proposed project.
For this reason, MRTT&T would work directly with strategic organizations in whatever
sector they may be: government, non-profit or for profit. In each of the project areas,
MRTT&T has identified private and public sector institutions that could respond to informal
sector needs and initiatives. The Govermnent would provide support services where it is in a
better position to do so, such as establishing an enabling policy and institutional environment.
Institutional strengthening activities initiated under the project, such as the Training and
Employment Fund, would provide a mechanism by which support from other donors could be
channeled to training and employment promotion activities in both public and private sector
institutions. Initially, the Government intends to offer financial assistance on a cost recovery
basis to encourage public and private sector training institutions and employers to respond to
informal sector training and technology development needs. Although such funds are at
present established and maitained through a levy on employers' payroll, this would not be a
realistic expectation for the informal sector until the current industrial training levy system is
modernized. The study and policy actions proposed under this Project will help fulfill this
requirement.
E. Activities of Other Major Donors
2.49 A number of donor agencies are currently active in supporting projects in technical
and vocational training directly benefitting the Jua Kali sector. However, as the examples of
donor projects illustrate, the investments are not guided by a coherent training policy.
Donors tend to agree that a comprehensive training policy is needed to justify further
investments. It should be noted, however, that donor support, experience and lessons learned
in the training sector informed the preparation of the proposed project.

2.50 DANIDA has been a major donor supporting craft level technical training and
informal sector/Jua Kali under the following programs: Industrial Training Program that has
established three Industrial Training Centres (Kisumu, Nairobi and Mombasa: the provision of
equipment, Technical Assistance, staff development and support to the Directorate of
Industrial Training (DIT) that manages the Industrial Training Levy management. DANIDA
is currently supporting programs for computer installation and a study on a possible
Management Information System aimed at revamping the DIT and the management of
data/information for the Industrial Training Levy. In addition, DANIDA will support the
three proposed policy studies on the trade testing system and Industrial Training Levy and
future management and utilization of the Levy funds. DANIDA has, in the past, supported
artisan and craft training programs at the Istitutes of Technology (former Harambee Institutes
of Technology) and Youth Polytechnics, as well as a pilot a Youth Polytechnics/Jua Kali
linkage program.
2.51  The Federal Republic Of Germany through the German Technical Cooperation (GTZ),
German Volunteer Service (GVS) and the Friedrich Ebert Foundation supports the Jua Kali
programs, e.g., Infrastructure Program and the Vocational Training Program in the Institutes
of Technology. The Friedrich Ebert Foundation supported the Jua Kali Federation and
provides technical assistance and training of Jua Kali Federation's comnmittee members on
organizational structures, legal issues, program planning, management, markedng, credit,
product development, members promotion, Government and donors relations (see Annex 5).
In addition, the Foundation has supported, through loans and credit guarantee programs
monitored by the Small Enterprise Finance Company (SEFCO), the promotion of micro- and
small enterprises. The Belgium Government has also provided some support to the
development of Jua Kali infrastructure and training.
2.52 USAID has provided funds to the Kenya Rural Enterprise Program (K-REP), a

give loans and technical assistance to other small enterprises development institutions.
USAID also supports K-MAP with fimds to provide business counselling to their on business

2.53   UNDP and ILO are supporting Jua Kali programs in the area of Entrepreneurship
Education in Technical Training Institutions and Youth Training and Employment Creation.
UNDP and UNIDO are supporting training for Jua Kalis, e.g., by establishing a National Jua

Women Textile Project at the Kenya Textile Training Institute in Nairobi with a branch at
Kimaffii IT in Nyeri.
2.54 ODA has supported the Kenya and Mombasa Polytechnics Project which over the
years provided the two national polytechnics with facilities, equipment, technical assistance
and staff training for craft and technician programs. Currently, ODA is undertakdng further
strengthening of the Management Information System by providing information technology
equipment to cover also the training needs, technical assistance, computer/development
officers and staff training. In addition, ODA has, in the past, funded the provision of training
packages, resource personnel and materials for the training of Jua Kalis at the CRT in tools
making and fabrication.
