F. Lessons from Previous Bank Involvement
2.55   A 1988 World Bank document on "Employment and Growth" recommended that
training opportunities be increased for the members of the labor force. On-the-job training
would be either provided through apprenticeship or industrial attachment or at a public or
private institution. Moreover, it was recommended that (a) policy-making focus more on the
provision of an appropriate institutional framework including an incentive system that would
enhance the scope for on-the-job training and skill development; (b) more attention be paid to
the traditional ways of skill acquisition like apprenticeship and family labor and how these
processes could be improved; and (c) new forms of delivering training and testing, i.e., post-
training and follow-up services, be explored. The B ink Report "Kenya Human Resources:
Improving Quality and Access" (1991) reconumends more cost sharing and cost-effective
measures in the use of educational facilities, equipment, materials and personnel. These
themes are taken up in the Bank's Country Economic Memorandum on Increasing
Employment Growth in Kenya (1993) and are addressed under the proposed project.26/
The CEM puts a particular emphasis on the capability and flexibility of the training system
which can respond to the skill needs of employers, including the informal sector workers. To
this end, more involvement of employers is suggested in determining the content and
provision of training and share in the costs. Its specific recommendations are completely
aligned with this project.
2.56 Informal Sector Training. Since 1966, since 1966, IDA's Kenya portfolio
comprised six Education Projects, four of which involved expansion of vocational and
technical education in the formal system. Informal sector training was never the main focus,
although it derived some benefits from these investments. The main focus of these
investments was employment training through the establishment of new technical schools and
institutes. More specifically, the First Education Project (Cr. 93-KE) supported the
constuction of two technical schools and assisted secondary schools with additional machinery
and teaching equipment to broaden their curricula with more practical subjects. While the
physical facilities were completed, although with a very large cost overrun, the technical
secondary school system was handicapped by weak programs due to a shortage of technically
qualified teachers. The Curriculum Development Center prepared technical education syllabi
but failed to produce pupil learning materials. The new secondary technical school suffered
from a frequent turnover of teachers including the headmaster, low standards and poor
management. In other project schools, the enrollment targets were not met and workshop
facilities were underutilized. Finally, some equipment supplied to vocational schools under
the Credit was seldom used or not at all due to the lack of qualified teachers or of parts for
foreign-supplied machines; the workshops were also too small and poorly equipped for the
newly introduced courses (see PPAR, 1974).
2.57   The Second Educadon Project (Cr. 185-KE) included assistance for the development
of agricultural education and training and the construction of one new secondary technical
school and the expansion and equipment of five secondary technical schools and one technical
institute. The main handicap was lack of teachers with industrial experience and pedagogical
training. Low salaries provided very little incentive for people with appropriate experience to
N     Wodmd Bank. Kenya-Humn Resources: Improving Quality and Access. Sector Report (1991). Employnent and
Growth in Kenya. Country Econoic Memorandum (1988).



X                           l21
accept a teaching post in a technical school. Expected employment targets for technical
school graduates were not realized and only marginallv better graduates were produiced than
graduates of the general secondary schools. About fifty percent of technical school graduates
did not readily find employment (see PCR. 1978 and PPAR, i979?.
2.58   The Fourth Education Project (Cr. 797-KE) provided support for a pilot project aimed
at improving equity in vocational education. 30 youth polytechnics (YP1s). originally known
as village polytechnics or craft training centers, were established and provided with equipnment
and small handtools. To encourage national coordination for thle YPs. the projccl assisted in
the expansion of the Karen Center for Research and Training (CRT). Poor communiication
among Government agencies and lack of clear Governmenit policy anid commiiiiitlmienit led to
very poor execution of this component. There were delays in the supply of' equipmelnt to the
YPs. The CRT, while well equipped, was virtually unutilized due to lack of staff as well as
clear policy while government direction and enthusiasmii for thlis institution,. whose role *vis-a-
vis the YPs, was not even clear (see PCR, 1985 and PPAR, 1987).
2.59   The Fifth Education Project (Cr. 1107-KE) provided assistance for training skilled
workers in the industrial sector through the expansion of Industrial Training Centers (ITCs) in
Kisumu and Mombasa. Although it is too early to have a full evaluation. there were strong
indications that provision of extensions to two ITCs had a positive impact on human resources
development through graduating technicians much in demand in the labor market (see PCR,
1992). However, there seems to be underutilization of capacity at both institutions.
2.60   In sum, although vocational investments have been high, they have not been part of a
broader strategy to address such important issues as relevance, cost efficiency, self-financing
and sustainability. This has resulted in a mismatch between training supply and skill demand,
especially by informal sector operators. underutilization of training capacity and an overall
weak financial position of the VET system. Other countries also faced similar constraints in
their training systems which has led to the preparation of a new Bank VET policy paper and
which calls for more training in the private sector, measures to improve the responsiveness
and efficiency of public training, and the development of a longer-term strategy for the
evolution of the Government's role in training.
2.61 Infrastructure development. IDA has provided two credits relevant to the proposed
infrastructure component: Nairobi Site and Service Project (Credit 543-KE) and Small-Scale
Industry Project (Credit 750-KE). Implementation of the first Credit suffered from disputes
over building and infrastructure standards, i.e., too rigid bye-laws which caused long delays.
Cost recovery fell short of its objectives. There were delays in establishing the management
unit and the project was extended from 5 to 7 1/2 years. The second Credit faced more
severe problems. Kenya Industrial Estates failed to implement the project due to the lack of
appropriate staff. They failed to collect bad debts and establish proper cooperation with its
field operators. Thus, only 50% of the Credit was utilized; only one of the eight sites
suggestet f^r development was used, feasibility stuaies on these sites being rejected by the
Bank. To ensure that the defects of the above mentioned Credits not be repeated, the
proposed infrastructure component would be experimental in terms of the method for
construction and ownership. It would be implemented on a pilot basis, ensuring proper
supervision and evaluation.



22
G. Rationale for IDA Assistance
2.62   The Bank's country strategy for Kenya identifies the dual imperative of rapid
employment creation and poverty alleviation as its main objectives. This strategy would entail
bolder implementation of an appropriate policy agenda given the prospects of large-scale
unemployment and substantial increases in the numbers of Kenyans living in absolute poverty.
Within this Bank/IDA framework of poverty alleviation, the current dialogue with the GOK
also reinforces the Government's plans to promote training and employment opportunities in
the informal sector, especially for the poor and marginal segments of society. In this context,
the proposed project responds directly to the Ndegwa report recommendation for enhancing
technology transfer and strengthening skills upgrading in the informal sector as a means to
improve the overall efficiency and productivity of Jua Kali operations at the enterprise level.
The "demand driven" approach, linking in-service/on-the-job training for existing
entrepreneurs and apprentices more closely to employment opportunities and improved
productivity, is a central feature of the proposed project. This approach also corresponds to
the new Bank Policy on Vocational and Technical Education. In fact, the proposed project
would provide an early opportunity to implement this new policy. Last but not least, Lhis
project corresponds directly to the Bank's strategy for the Africa region, 27/ ,hich
emphasizes the dynamism and importance the informal sector plays in Africasn economies. It
aims at developing the informal sector by promoting access to technology related training and
institutional support within the framework of a more favorable policy environment.
27/    E. K. Jaycox (February 1993). Africa: From Stagnation to Recovery, World Bank, Washington, DC.



23
III. THE PROPOSED PROJECT
A. Project Concept and Objectives
3.1   The long term aims of the project are to enhance entrepreneurial development in the
private sector and, more specifically, reduce constraints to employment promotion and income
enhancement in the micro- and small enterprise (informal) sector in Kenya. The project
would target manufacturing enterprises with 1-50 workers and make provision for increasing
women's representation in this non-traditional sector, principally in major urban and peri-
urban areas of Kenya.
3.2   To accomplish the above, the project has three main components designed to: (a)
develop and implement policies enabling the entrepreneurial environment; (b) provide access
to skill training and appropriate technology for micro- and small enterprises and facilitate
technological innovation in the sector; and, (c) improve the operational and managerial
capacity of institutions and programs that support the sector's development. MRTT&T would
use private sector contractors, training and research institutions, Jua Kali Associations and
employers as the primary agents to promote and implement the proposed project.
B. Policy Actions
3.3    As the first step to foster an enabling policy environment for the informal sector,
various barriers to entry, operation and exit would be removed or simplified, e.g.,
registration, licensing, accessing urban land to Jua Kali operators, building standards, and
other outdated business regulatory laws. The Vagrancy, Nuisance and Chiefs Authority Acts
are already under review by the Commission on Criminal and Penal Procedures. Other
policies, which especially affect the broader economy, may be addressed through the PFP
(Policy Framework Paper) and future private sector reform and financial sector adjustment
operations. As for tlhe patent system, the Industrial Property Act was already erActed in
December, 1989 to make it more relevant to the Jua Kali sector by introducing utility
models, a type of "petty patents". While Annex 2 provides a comprehensive list of the legal
and regulatory constraints, the proposed Letter of Informal Sector Policy (LISP) and time
bound Action Plan (Annex 3) indicates the policies to be addressed under the proposed
project. As a condition prior to negotiation the GOK provided a Draft Letter of Informal
Sector Policy and Time-bound Action Plan (para. 6.2(a)). As a condition of Board
presentation. the GOK would provide a signed Letter of Informal Sector Policy and Time-
Bound Action Plan (para. 6.4(a)). As a condition of Disbursement on the civil works and
building materials Drocurement categories of the infrastructure component. the GOK would
submit the following draft bills to Parliament. according to the Letter of Informal Sector
Policy and Action Plan: the Registration of Business Names Act. the Trade Licensing Act. the
Local Government Adoptive By-Laws (building standards). and the Electric Power Act, as
well as the Vagrancv. Nuisance. and Chief's Authority Acts. (para. 6.6(a)).
3.4   To assist in the development of a long-term National Training Strategy more
responsive to productive sector training needs, the Government would prepare an Action Plan
based on (i) an assessment of the Ind"lstrial Training Levy scheme, and (ii) an evaluation of
the trade test and certification system, and (iii) a policy review process for skills training.
These would result in a draft National Training Strategy elaborating, among others, future
objectives, organization and management, as well as financing and content (institutions,



t                         24
curricula and certification) of the future training systems. The further development and
implementation of a National Training Strategy would be an outcome of the Institutional
Development Component of the project under the Policy Analysis, Monitoring and Evaluation
sub-component. Both policy analysis capacity and the implementation of the Project Training
Fund would facilitate project sustainability and longer term sectoral development. For
instance, the Project Training Fund design provides a model of a demand driven skill training
system embodying the principles proposed for the National Training Strategy: (a) relevance
(demand-driven), (b) quality and efficiency, and (c) financial sustainability (i.e., budget
allocation would be competitive and gradually shift from subsidy to cost sharing) allowing
training institutions more autonomy for financing. There would be enhanced diversification
and balance among private and public training provision and a new partnership between the
public and private sector guaranteeing the full participation for Jua Kali entrepreneurs and
employees (as individuals and througn their associations) in training and technology policy
and program decisions and subsequent implementation.
3.5   The Industrial Levy study would recommend how to make the levy system financially
sustainable and more responsive to the skill upgrading needs of productive sectors. The trade
test review would suggest ways to improve the standards and quality of skill training and
attendant certification and legitimization. The recommendations would become part of a
long-term National Training Strategy (See Annex 3 and Action Plan). As a condition prior to
negotiations the GOK provided terms of reference for the recruitment of external auditors to
audit the FY 93 Training Levy Funds (para. 6.2(c)). At negotiationis, the GOK provided
assurances that an assessment of the National Industrial Training Levy system and t_e trade
testing and certification system would be undertaken and a detailed Action Plan for
modemnizing these. including amendment of relevant acts if necess_a, would be drafted by
AuguLst 30. 1995. A policy review process for skills training and a draft National Training
Strategy would be cmmleted by December 30, 1995 (para. 6.3(a)). As a condition of Board
Presentation. the G0K would nrovide tender documents and advertisemnent for the recruitmnent
of external auditors to audit the FY 93 Training Levy Funds (para. 6.4(b)). As a condition of
credit effectiveness, the GOK would emloy externa auditors to audit the FY 93 Training
Levy Funds and provide their comnleted audit reports with recommended remedies for the FY
93 TrndninI Levy Funds (para. 6.5(a)).
C. Project Description
3.6   The following section describes the three investment components of the proposed
project and provides details of their different sub-components that address the various
constraints (such as market failures) to micro- and small enterprise development.
Component 1. Micro- and Small Enterpnse Traimne Fund (US$11.5 million)
3.7   This component consists of two sub-components to provide incentives for skill
upgrading and technology development for micro- and small enterprises and for diversifying
and improving public and private sector training capacity, including enterprise-based training.
Up to 60,000 informal sector entrepreneurs and workers, representing about 50 percent of the
manufacturing sector, would benefit from the services provided under this component (see
para.5.3).



1                         25
3.8    The project would establish a Micro- and Small Enterprise Training Fund and make a
contribution of US$11.5 million to its funding. By combining two competitive approaches to
funding training - a voucher program and a contract training scheme -- the Fund
addresses specific financial and quality issues related to reorienting the training system to a
demand-driven system. The voucher program would introduce consumer choice, enabling Jua
Kalis to purchase the training they want. Since the vouchers would be distributed among
entrepreneurs with low incomes, they would presumably benefit by a net real income increase
in the future. The contract scheme would encourage public and private institutions to provide
relevant (i.e., market- and skill driven) training to infonral sector clients on a competitive
basis. Similar approaches have been used effectively to promote market and employer
incentives to develop (upgrade) worker skills and production technologies in other countries,
notably in the Philippines and Singapore. 28/ Experience with the Singapore Skills
Development Fund provides evidence that vouchers can provide micro and small enterprises
with incentives to purchase training which they normally do not do.
3.9    The Project Training Fund would develop financing mechanisms (i.e.. a Jua Kali
training fund) as well as experience and lessons to be incorporated in the modernization of the
Industrial Training Levy scheme and thus enable project sustainability. This process is likely
to involve consolidation and privatization of training, facilitating a larger role for the privatc
sector and enterprises in skills training, especially regarding training facilities and cost
sharing, and improving mechanisms linking the Government, industry and workers. Training
programs would be convenient, reasonably priced and of limited duration. Trainers would be
familiar with non-formal and trade specific training approaches. To make economic sense
training would improve existing productive activity.29/
3.10   This component would be gender sensitive in several ways: training sub-components
would be flexible and targeted and make provision for increasing women's representation in
the lucrative manufacturing sector. Particular attention would be paid to women's access to
skills training and technology suitable for upgrading their enterprises or for bringing them into
thie sector. For instance, depending on the training sub-component, between 10% and 50% of
basic, entry-level technical skill training should be targeted to women's entry into this sector.
To this end, several vouchers could be awarded to one individual to cover longer-term
enterprise-based attachments. In order to sensitize female secondary school students to enter
non-traditional technical fields of productive work, this component would support ongoing
outreach efforts of the Training and Gender units both at the MRTT&T and Jua Kali
Federation through the inclusion of gender awareness in the proposed staff development
programs, especially regarding leadership and entrepreneurship training and training materials
development.
3.11   The Training Fund would be governed by a Project Steering Committee (a
government-private sector partnership between MRTT&T and the Jua Kali
Federation/Associations). The Training and Gender Unit (TGU) of the Directorate of Applied
Technology (DAT), MRTT&T in conjunction with the Jua Kali Federation and local
28/    Vocadonal Training on the Threshold of the 1990s. Volume I and H. PHREE Background Paper Series 91/35. The
Wodd Bank (Febuary, 1991). See also draft report on Policy Incentives for Employer-Based Training by Sachi Takeda (World
Bank EA3PH, June, 1992); Vocational Training Project: Philippines. SAR 3750b-PH, 1982.
29/    Skills for Productivity. Vocational Education and Training in Developing Countries.
John Middleton, Adrian Ziderman, Arvil van Adams. A World Bank Book, 1993.



t                         26
Associations would coordinate Fund management contracts. This process would be facilitated
by parallel staff development in such areas as training program management and training
needs assessment. The contract training scheme sub-projects would be executed by public or
private institutions which must compete for Fund resources under pre-defined qualifiwation
and selection criteria. For the voucher program, the local Jua Kali Associations would act as
match-making brokers with technical support from contractors. The contractor would provide
capacity building of the local Jua Kali Associations until which point in time they can fully
undertake implementation responsibility. (See Annex 9 for a detailed description of the
Training Fund programs as well as inplementation guidelines and modalities). As a condition
of credit effectiveness the GOK woul'- obtain Cabinet Approval for the establishment of the
Project Training Fund (para. 6.5(b)).
Sub-component la: Training Voucher Program (US$6.1 million)
3.12 The Fund would support the financing of a demand driven voucher program to pay
for short-term skill upgrading courses and enterprise-based training attachmnents benefiting
entrepreneurs and artisans. Local Jua Kali Associations would manage the program under the
supervision of MRTT&T and Jua Kali Federation. Training services would be selected by the
beneficiaries themselves. Association members (individuals or groups) would purchase the
skill training in demand wherever available. The voucher program would selectively p-omote
sub-projects that emphasize methods of improving micro-and small enterprise product-; ity
through training and upgrading in areas such as technical skills, business management skills,
product design and diversification, product marketing, standards application and adaptation,
sub-contracting, and choice and use of appropriate technologies. Priority would be given to
training master craftsmen who could then improve both the informal apprenticeship system
and product development. Other criteria for selection in the voucher program would be the
Jua Kali operator's business background as well as the growth potential of the particular
trade. The number of vouchers granted annually to Jua Kali Associations is also determined
by the criteria for sub-project selection outlined in Annex 9.
3.13   The voucher program aims to distribute up to 30,000 vouchers, on average 6,000 per
year, at a unit cost (per voucher) of US$150 for management training (total 10,000 vouchers)
and US$200 for technical skill upgrading (total 20,000 vouchers), including trade testing II or
I. A typical skill upgrading course would last about 2 to 6 weeks (including enterprise-based,
evening, on-site and residential courses). Vouchers would be used for public and private
sector institutions and enterprises that provide access to skill upgrading and technology
developmnent to the micro- and small enterprise sector. Since vouchers give clients the
purchasing power to select among private or public institutions of their choice, they have a
built-in price mechanism (pricing of the end product). This approach to skill upgrading is
particularly useful to prevent oversupply of any one type of training.
3.14   Under the voucher program, cost-sharing is expected from the beneficiaries in terns
of opportunity costs incurred by the training (i.e., lost income, transport and supplies). In the
initial stage, the Training Fund would finance ninety percent of the voucher and remaining ten
percent by the trainees, in order to develop the program and stimulate demand for skill
upgrading among micro- and small enterprise entrepreneurs and employees, many of whom
are marginalized poor urban workers. However, at a later stage the Jua Kali Associations
and/or indiviudal artisans would be eventually expected to contribute to the full cost of the
training program to ensure its sustainability. Training modalities would be flexible (i.e., easy
access through outreach programs), in order to minimize opportnity costs.



1                        27
Sub-component lb: Contract Training Scheme (US$S.4 million)
3.15   The Training Fund would support the financing of a supply-side training contracts
scheme to approved public and private sector training providers on a competitive basis.
Sub-projects would be eligible for funding which: respond to the relevant skill requirements
of Jua Kalis, both technical and management skills, and are gender-sensitive, i.e., competitive
in skill areas where women are actively involved and pro-active in skill areas where they are
underrepresented. The upgrading of the training capability of contractors, such as training of
trainers would also be included in any given training contract. Special emphasis would be
placed on support to private sector enterprise-based training (a target floor of up to 50% of
the contract awards) in an effort to improve the capacity el both public and private training
institutions through equal opportunity as well as competitiveness. Employers who provide on-
site skill upgrading can receive reimbursement of up to 100 percent (within approved ceilings)
for upgrading training equipment related to skills upgrading and technology development.
3.16   The contract scheme aims to award up to 1,500 training contracts for various courses
and services on a competitive basis. An average of 300 sub-projects are expected to be
submittod to the Fund annually, ranging from enterprise-based skill upgrading to advanced
tailor-made courses. Group-based training would comprise 10-15 trainees each for technical
training and 15-25 trainees each for management training. A total of 30,000 entrepreneurs
and employees are expected to benefit from contract training.
3.17   As a partial cost recovery strategy, beneficiaries are expected to pay minimal training
fees, similar to those currently charged by the ITCs to Jua Kali artisans. Once the training
schemes have proven themselves, flexible but competitive fee paying arrangements would be
worked out between the Jua Kali Associations, their members and the training institutions (in
consultation with PTTOs/YTOs). The underlying assumption of this approach is that
demanding fees (even non-economic ones) for services provides an indication that the services
are of value. Therefore, it can be expected that training fees would adjust to demand if
information about costs and training outcomes is available to clients. In this way, training
institutions can be held accountable for price and quality of training available in the market
place. Contracts would be based on the number of trainees trained and active recruitment of
women. They would be limited to maximum amounts of money (i.e., US$3,600 average
amount per contract/course. However, cumulative financing for any given enterprise or
institution would be determined by market forces and quality indicators, given the competitive
basis of the skill upgrading schemes.
3.18   To ensure demand responsiveness, special studies/surveys would be undertaken in the
Jua Kali Federation/Associations network (presently about 400 associations representing some
80,000 enterprises and workers) to assess skill upgrading needs of managers and operators,
and production/skill specialization and capability of potential enterprises as training providers.
Training institutions would need to be identified for those manufacturing sub-sectors where
women are now represented, such as textiles and foods. The well-known and established
Kenyan women's enterprises, especially in textiles, should be considered in identifying and
selecting enterprise-based training sites. This data base would be utilized to create directories
anu data bases for efficient information sharing and match-making between training seekers
and providers. The Training Units (MRTT&T and Jua Kali Federation/Associations) would
maintain directories of public and private training providers, including enterprises, and would
advise training institutions and enterprises in the preparation of project proposals and, where
necessary, advise in their initial execution.



t                         28
3.19  The Training and Gender Unit (TGU) would prepare terms of reference and contracts
required for sub-project execution and ensure that sub-project applications submitted to the
TGU meet allocation criteria, such as training and technology needs identified by the
appropriate unit to be established at MRTT&T/PDU. The institution's capacity to deliver the
proposed services and maintain auditable accounts should be clearly evident. Training
methods should be suitable to an adult population. Training providers would have to be open
to the notion of training women and making any necessary modifications in the design and
implementation of courses in areas of manufacturing where few women currently participate.
Institutions should be willing to establish links with Jua Kali associations and offer acceptable,
affordable and equitable skill improvement courses to those Jua Kalis nearest the institutions.
In the case of enterprise-based training, to prevent financing of productive work that would
have taken place in any event, eligibilit.1 criteria would be clearly spelled out and closely
monitored (see Annex 9).
Comnolent 2. Technoloe Development and Pflot Infrasructure (US$5.6 milHon)
3.20 This component comprises two sub-components: (i) a Technology
Information/Innovation and Research Program would stimulate information exchange and
innovation in product development through assistance to a technology information clearing
function, technology study tours, and marketing exhibitions; and a small pilot program of
adaptive technology research grants, awarded on a competitive basis, to promote the
indigenous capacity for the research, design, and commercialization of new products and
production technologies for the informal sector. These programs would be implemented by
the Jua Kali Federation/Associations and other privately contracted organizations; and (ii) an
Infrastucture Development program would supply Jua Kalis with secure worksites and access
to appropriate infrastructure, e.g. power, water, sanitation, refuse collecdon and roads.
Development would take place on an experimental/pilot basis on sites in Kisumu and
Mombasa where land has already been secured for Jua Kali from local authorities. Equitable
access to these benefits would be closely monitored through collecting appropriate monitoring
and evaluation data (see Annex 17).
Sub-Component 2a Techology Infonnation/Innovation and Research Program (US$2.8
milion)
3.21 This sub-component would provide the necessary support to facilitate innovation in the
informal sector in product design and quality improvement. The target group for this
component would be owner-managers of micro- and small enterprises in existence for over
five years, who have basic technological experience and are likely to be innovators. The
proposed activities facilitate this requirement with a view to stimulating significant sustained
growth in employment and incomes within the sector.
3.22   A product and processes clearing function as well as a marketing or exhibitions
outreach p- gram would be established to gather and dissemnnate information on new products
and prodi .ion technologies to informal sector entrepreneurs through publications,
advertisements, exhibitions, workshops, and other appropriate media. Innovation in product
development would be stimulated through demonstration, information exchanges, and
participation in trade and technology fairs, and training. The proposed project would assist in
the development of the program through the provision of equipment, training, and technical
assistance (see Annex 14).



t                       29
3.23   About 200 technology study visits for selected innovator/entrepreneurs to East and
South Asia and Africa would be sponsored. These visits would be organized for promising
Jua Kali manufacturers and last up to 30 days. The visits could also include short attachments
with foreign enterprises. In addition to providing for the transportation, per diem, and related
program operating costs, the project would provide a technology procurement allowance of up
to $300 per participant to purchase a new piece of equipment/technology for utilization in
Kenya, while the costs exceeding this amount would be paid by the participant. There would
be no import tax duties on this euqipment up to KSH. 20 million in value. The participants
would be required to submit reports of the study visit upon the completion of their visits and
to attend technology and marketing exhibiitons at least once to share their experiences. They
also may be required to participate in the training activities from time to time. This program
would be contracted out to matchmaker/brokers utilizing ICB procurement procedures. The
program would be closely monitored to allow for reallocation of funds depending on actual
performance and actual verifiable expenses.
3.24   Technology and marketing exhibitions would have marketing and technology exchange
functions somewhat like the Annual Canton Trade Fair. Provision would be made through
-Ile project to organize and finance encounters among Jua Kali from various corners of Kenya
on a semi-annual basis in rotating locations, to exhibit and market their products and
production technologies. The four main interest groups involved in such exhibitions will be:
entrepreneurs-innovators, potential buyers/sub-contractors, equipment and raw material
suppliers, and other Jua Kali owner-managers. While the public will be welcome on an
admission (cost recovery fee basis), the fair will be centered around technology exchange
opportunity identification and trade. The project would finance rental and preparation of
exhibition facilities, information and promotion of the fair, workshops and other semi-formal
exchanges during the fair, and selected assistance to transport the most needy/worthy
entrepreneur/innovators and their wares. As the exhibitions gradually shape during the
project implementation period, rental of exhibition booths, and other cost-sharing measures
would be progressively implemented so as to eventually put the exhibitions on a self-financing
basis.
3.25   The project would finance about 50 applied technology research grants over the five
year project period, on a competitive basis, to promote the design, development and
commercialization of new products and production technologies for the micro- and small
enterprise sector and thereby leverage and develop the existing applied technology research
capacity in Kenya. A number of private and public research institutions/individuals such as
the mechanical engineering department at the Mombasa Polytechnic, the Center for Applied
Technology at Jomo Kenyatta University, the Kenya Industrial Research Development
Institute (KIRDI), inter alia, are already developing new products and technologies.
However, they have loose connections to the market (especially the informal sector market)
for such technologies and limited venture resources to expand their applied research efforts.
Proposals would be judged on the basis of their technical strength, cost-effectiveness, and
commercialization prospects. The proposal guidelines, promotion, vetting and overall
management will be undertaken by MRTT&T (see Annex 10).



t                          30
Sub-Component 2b: Pilot Infrastructure Development (US$2.8 million)
3.26   This sub-component would be designed to provide Jua Kalis with legal and secure
tenure to worksites and to facilitate theiv access to appropriate infrastructure. Development of
infrastructure (sites and services) would be carried out on a pilot basis in two major cities
(Moinbasa and Kisumu), given the Bank's inexperience in this type of activities in Kenya.
Sites would be selected with a condition being that clear land title has been already secured
for Jua Kali associations or individual enterprises from local authorities. As a condition of
neaotiation the GOK provided evidence of (i) bonafide membership (registration list) in the
Jua Kali Association: (ii) clear individual or Association ownership (copies of letter of
allotment purchase receipt. and leasehold title) of sites (Kisumu and Mombasa) designated for
infrastructure provision under the proiect (para. 6.2(f)).
3.27   The infrastructure programs would be implemented by the Jua Kali
Federation/Associations with managerial, technical, and capacity building assistance from
other privately contracted organizations. They would link up with the training to provide an
'incubator" style package to promote the development and graduation into the formal sector
of micro- and small enterprises on an experimental basis. In the towns, the total 500
workplaces of three different sizes will be developed: 150 workplaces of 6mx6m, 225
workplaces of 6m x 10m and 125 workplaces of 10in x 16m. Each of the workplaces will
have individual water, sewerage, electrical connections. The sites will also be provided with
access roads, public toilets, refuse collection facilities, and drainage. Given that Jua Kalis are
skilled artisans, self-help construction methods will be adopted in addition to hiring full-time
professional workers. The workplaces would be allocated to beneficiaries only after they are
built to full completion.
3.28 Jua Kali Associations, handling sites and related infrastructure, would establish a
revolving fund with attendant user charges for recovering infrastructure investments and other
costs. Rents and charges collected would be deposited into two separate accounts, Jua Kali
Operational Fund and Jua Kali Infrastructure Fund. About 15 percent of the monthly
revenue, deposited into the Jua Kali Operational Fund, will be used for management and
maintenance of the Infrastructure. This amount would be adjusted according to ages of the
work places and worksites. The balance, deposited into the Jua Kali Infrastructure Fund,
would be invested in short- and medium-term securities and would upon rzquest be ploughed
back into development of additional Jua Kali sites or if necessary, provision of credit
facilities. The Jua Kali Operational Fund would be managed by Local Jua Kali Associations
with contracted technical assistance, while the Jua Kali Infrastructure Fund by the Project
Steering Committee. At negotiations. the GOK Drovided assurances that annual audits of
these two accounts by independent auditors acceptable to IDA would be carried out and their
audit reports would be available to IDA (para. 6.3 (c)). Baseline data would be collected as
part of the project monitoring and evaluation sub-component (see project monitoring and
evaluation below) to ensure that lessons are drawn on the relative impact of the training,
technology, infrastructure and institutional/policy package on productivity, incomes and
employment for replication purposes.
3.29 This sub-component would be managed by local Jua Kali Associations under the
supervision of the MRTT&T and Jua Kali Federation. The establishment of a solid
institutional structure and competent management is essential to ensure the successful
implementation of this first Bank supported infrastructure project for the Jua Kali sector,
considering that selection and allocation of sites in the project is politically sensitive. A



31
management plan would be designed for each Jua Kali Association and management training
for Jua Kali Association representatives would be provided under the Staff Development
component, as will be described in the next section. In addition, technical assistance tnrough
management consultant firms would be provided in the start up phase (Annex 8 describes the
infrastructure component in detail).
Component 3. Institutional Develoument (US$2.1 million)
3.30   This component would help develop a new partnership between the public and private
sector guaranteeing the full participation for Jua Kali entrepreneurs and workers (as
individuals and through their associations) in training and technology policy and program
decisions and subsequent implementation. To accomplish this, two sub-components would
support the establishment of: (i) a staff development program for MRTT&T (based upon an
organizational study and staff redeployment plan) and Jua Kali Federation/Associations; and
(ii) a monitoring and evaluation system to make the training system more quality conscious,
responsive to the needs of the export sector, and financially sustainable. This component
would also support the development of a national training strategy which is crucial for project
sustainability and longer term sectoral development.
Sub-component 3a: Staff Development (US$0.7 million)
3.31   The government-private sector partnership between the MRTT&T and the Jua Kali
Federation/Associations would comprise the chief executing agents for the project. The
purposes of this sub-component would be to: (a) undertake an organizational review of
MRTT&T and the Jua Kali Federation resulting in a staff redeployment and development plan
requi.ed for project implementation; and (b) train MRTT&T/DAT and it's a regional staff
(PTTO, YTO training officers) as well as Jua Kali representatives in the skills required for the
effective oversight and management of the proposed training, technology and infrastructure
components. The main goal is to develop an implementation strategy with substantial
beneficiary involvement in the management of project activities. To this end, there would be
a particular focus on assisting training and gender unit staff in order to facilitate more
equitable distribution of project benefits and enhance monitoring of female participation. This
would require that both Training and Gender Units have suitable staff before project start-up.
At negotiations. the GOK provided the assurance that an organizational review of MRTT&T
and the Jua Kali Federation. would be completed by December 31. 1994 and a subsequent
staff redeployment and development plan required for successful project implementation.
would be submitted to IDA by June 30. 1995 (para. 6.3(b)).
3.32   Staff would be trained in such areas as coordination of the voucher program brokering
(match-making between trainees and skill-upgrading providers); sub-project appraisal of the
contract training proposals from the public and private sector providers with the assistance of
training contract support services and the local Jua Kali Associations; and oversight of
contract implementation of the staff development (capacity building of the Ministry and
Federation/Association) programs, management of the contractors and related private sector
consultant contract services (See Annex 4 for implementation organizational Chart of the key
executing bodies, MRTT&T and the Jua Kali Federation/Associations (national and district
levels).



32
3.33   The following staff development courses would be designed and implemented, but
not limited to these: (i) project management; (ii) procurement and contract administration; (iii)
financial management; (iv) monitoring/evaluation; (v) leadership and entrepreneurship
development; (vi) training program management; (vii) technology program management; (viii)
planning and financing micro- and small-scale enterprises; and (ix) training materials
development. These courses would last approximately five days each and enroll about 25
participants. It is estimated that a total of 50 courses would be organized for MRTT&T and
Jua Kali Federation/Associations (see Annex 15 for a detailed staff development plan).
Sub-component 3b: Policy Analysis, Monitoring and Evaluation (US$1.4 million)
3.34   The major objective of this sub-component is to make the informal sector training
system more quality conscious, responsive to the needs of the export sector, demand driven,
and financially sustainable. Policy analysis would focus on the implementation, management
and sustainability of the Project Training Fund. For instance, the assessment of the Training
Levy System, with an eye toward improving its financial sustainability, would be a critical
activity of this sub-component. Subsequently, the modernization of the Industrial Training
Levy study and the development of a draft National Training Strategy would be important
outcomes of the project, given that the levy reform and training strategy are crucial for
project sustainability and longer term sectoral development.
3.35 The evaluation of the trade testing and certification system would also be financed
under this sub-component. Integrating indigenous and informal technologies into the trade
test system would be an important means to facilitate modernization of the informal sector and
to advance technologies in micro- and small enterprises. Strengthening the trade testing
system would also improve the quality of training. To this end, the DIT and other support
services would be provided with the necessary resources, such as consultancies and
equipment. The changes proposed for the trade test system would be closely linked to the
development of skill training programs. Reformed trade tests would reflect a broader range
of job profiles and technologies found in the informal sector. Employers (including Jua Kali)
representing the various trades would participate in defining the job profiles and in updating
the tests. Trade testing would not only be used to measure skldi development, but also to
provide the basis for clearly defined trade skills. As a condition prior to negotiations the
GOK provided draft terms of reference for trade test and industrial training lev studies and
policy review process for skills trainine (para.6.2(e)).
3.36 In addition to the above, regular assessments of trainee skill and technology needs,
and training provider capacity would be undertaken by Jua Kali Associations on an annual
basis. This exercise would establish the data base to publish national directories for purposes
of matching/brokering prospective Jua Kali trainees with training providers and technology
information services (see Amex 11). Monitoring national training capacity at MRTT&T
would further the strategic planning functions of the Ministry including the implementation of
government training policy.
3.37   The project would assist in developing a management information and monitoring
system at MRTT&T with a feedback system linked to local Jua Kali Associations, since
project activities attempt to respond to informal sector needs. The project would finance the
equipment, related training and technical assistance necessary to effectively strengthen such
functions and create new ones at the MRTT&T. Such a system would provide guidance and
criteria for funding specific training interventions. The policy reforms and action programs



33
would be implemented under the LISP and monitored by the project monitoring system (See
Annex 16 for detailed descriptions of project monitoring and impact evaluation indicators).
3.38  Project impact evaluation would involve the development of a data base on informal
sector enterprises by strengthening an existing survey, Small Scale Enterprise Survey
conducted by the Central Bureau of Statistics (CBS) (The Welfare Monitoring Survey will
provide baseline data for project monitoring as outlined in Annex 19). These functions would
be strengthened in collaboration with the Ministries of Labor and Planning.

IV. PROJECT COSTS AND FINANCING
A. Costs
4.1    Sumrnary of Project Costs. The total cost of the project is estimated at about
US$24.2 million, with a base cost of US$20.9 million. Price contingencies, estimated at
US$2.3 million, assume an annual domestic inflation rate of 20 percent for the first year (10
percent for the second year and 5% thereafter) and a foreign inflation rate of 2.8 percent.
Physical contingencies are estimated at US$1.0 million. IDA financing would be
approximately 90 percent of total project costs or about US$21.8 million. The Government
and Jua Kali Associations would finance approximately 10 percent of total project costs or
about US$2.4 million. Items imported directly for the project would be exemnpt from duties
and taxes (Detailed costs are presented in Annex 12 and summarized in table 4.1 below).

4.2    Base Cost Estimates. The project base costs are estimated in June 1993 prices,
calculated in US dollars and converted to Kenya Shillings at the prevailing exchange rate of
Ksh 71.0/US$1.0. Civil works and building material costs are based on current market prices
made available by the construction industry. Unit costs of furnishings, equipment, vehicles
and other materials are based on contract prices for similar items currently being awarded
under the Universities Investment Project (Credit 2309-KE). Technical assistance costs for
experts recruited internationally include costs of salaries, allowances and airfares, and average
US$10,500 per staff month. Services of local consultants average US$1,500 per staff month.
Short-term training overseas, including travel and subsistence costs, is estinated at US$5,000
per month.
4.3    The foreign exchange component, estimated at US$8.6 million or 36 percent of the
total project cost excluding duties and taxes, has been calculated as follows: a) 62 percent for
contracted civil works and building materials; b) 100 percent for equipment; c) 100 percent
for vehicles, overseas training and study tours; d) 60 percent for management training; e) 10
percent for other staff training; e) 80 percent for equipment operation and maintenance; f) 30
percent for studies and services of local consulting firms; g) 20 percent for building operation
and maintenance; and h) zero percent for staff salaries and per diem.
4.4 Physical contingencies of US$1.0 million equivalent have been estimated at five
percent of base costs for all categories of expenditure except for technical assistance (experts,
studies and overseas training) which has been estimated at ten percent of base cost. Price
contingencies of US$2.3 million equivalent are based on a seven year project implementation
period commencing July, 1994.
4.5    Recurrent Costs. Incremental operating costs under the project are estimated at
US$2.9 million with a breakdown as follows: consumables (US$1.3); maintenance (US$0.2);
and travel and per diem (US$1.4).
B. Finaing Plan
4.6   Project Financing. The total costs of US$24.2 million equivalent, excluding duties
and taxes, would be financed by the Government, Jua Kali Associations and IDA.
4.7    The IDA Credit of US$21.8 million (SDR  million) would cover about 90 percent of
total project cost or 100 percent of the foreign exchange cost and 86 percent of the local cost
net of duties and taxes. The Govermment and JKA contribution of US$2.4 million equivalent
would finance about 10 percent of the total project cost. About US$ 0.5 million equivalent,
or 2 percent of total project cost, would be contributed by Jua Kali Associations and private
individuals towards the constuction (materials and labor) of storage and distribution facilities
at the Jua Kali development locations in Mombasa and Kisumu (The financing plan is shown
in Table 4.2.).

4.8    Counterpart Funding and Project Sustainabilitv. The Government has recently been
experiencing difficulties in making adequate counterpart funding available to some donor-
assisted projects. This is a macro problem, arising from the low Government revenue effort
and specific aims to decrease the budget deficit to levels that can be sustained by foreign and
non-inflationary domestic financing. Government's aim for 1991-1994 to achieve an output
growth of above 5 %; reduce inflation to the average level forecast of 5 %; and reduce the
external current account deficit to a sustainable level of 4 %-5 % of GDP will absorb much of
its resources which could have a constraining effect on the supply of counterpart funds for the
future. Bank Country Implementation Reviews (CIR), year-by-year budget reviews and a
mid-term review in 1997 would focus on this issue to ensure consistency between project
activities and that adequate counterpart funding would be available.
4.9    Long-term sustainability of the project investments, following project completion,
depends on developments both outside, and within, the sector. Outside the sector, it is
expected that appropriate financial and structural policies will reduce inflation (currentiy at
20%), increase private investment (fall of 0.6 % from 11% in last three years), and reduce
the fiscal deficit (7% of GDP). Within the sector and through the project, Government would
reduce discriminatory policy environmental constraints, enable better access to information
and technology to producers, improve access to credit via commercial banks, provide an
adequate infrastructure base from which to operate, provide better access to more dynamic
marikets (inputs and sub-contracting) and improve the supply of raw materials to micro-
producers. This will generate income, lower production costs, achieve better quality control,
create employment and have a positive impact on the reduction of poverty within the informal
sector.
C. Disbursements
4.10   Disbursements. The Bank disbursement profile for all sectors in Kenya is seven
years. The project would therefore be completed by June 30, 2001 and its closing date would
be December 31, 2001. The proposed Credit allocation is shown in Table 4.3 and estimated
disbursement schedule in Annex 13 along with the project and country disbursement profiles.
4.11   All disbursements will be made in line with Bank Guidelines and will operate under
procedu--- , ceptable to IDA. Disbursements will be fully documented except for payments
for contracts and purchase orders less than US$50,000 equivalent and expenditures for local
training programs and operating costs, which would be disbursed on the basis of statements of
expenditure (SOEs). SOEs will be certified by the Head of Finance and Accounts Department
(FAD) in the MRTT&T, who will confirm that these are in agreement with the ledger
accounts, copies of which will accompany the claims. The ledger accounts and other
supporting documentation will, however, be retained and made available for review by IDA
supervision missions. Wherever possible, all withdrawal applications should be aggregated in
batches of not less than US$25,000 equivalent.

4.12 Special Account. A foreign exchange Special Account of USD 1.5 million
(approximately four months of eligible expenditures), funded by IDA, will be established in a
commercial bank and operated by the Finance and Accounts Department (FAD) of MRTT&T.
Such account will be maintained in accordance with sound and generally recognized
accounting principles and practices, satisfactory to the Bank, to reflect the performance and
position of the project. All payments of USD 150,000 or less would be channeled through
the Special Account. The minimum size application for direct payment or issuance of Special
Commitment shall be USD 150,000 equivalent. The Finance and Accounts Department (FAD)
will submit replenishment requests, accompanied by full documentation except for those items
eligible for disbursement on the basis of certified SOEs, on a monthly basis or whenever the
Special Account is diminished by one third, whichever comes first. The Government will
make available its contribution through the approved development estimates, as the case may
be, for items to be financed out of the Special Account at the same time as withdrawals are
made from the Special Account. All requests for replenishment of the Special Account will
be consolidated by FAD and countersigned by the Head of Department.
4.13   Audits. Project accounts would be audited annually covering all expenditures related
to the project and with particular attention to and including separate opinions for (a)
expenditures reimbursed agairnst SOEs, and (b) the administration of the Special Account.



39
Reports will include a detailed statement on the adequacy of the accounting system and
internal controls. At negotiations. the GOK provided assurances that annual audits of special
account and SOEs bv independent auditors acceptable to IDA would be carried out and that
all audited accounts would be made available to IDA within six months of the close of each
fiscal year (para. 6.3(c)). The auditors opinion and report would be countersigned by the
Auditor General. Project accounting, financial reporting and auditing arrangements will
provide adequate and timely information to the Bank for supervision of disbursements and the
project generally.
D. Procurement
4.14   Procurement arrangements for items financed by IDA are given in Table 4.4.
Procurement of all goods and services funded under the project and review of procurement
documents shall be done in accordance with Bank Group Guidelines (issued May 1992).
World Bank sample documents for procurement of civil works and goods will be followed
under ICB procurement, and all procurement arrangements will be coordinated by the
DAT/ATU.
4.15   Procedures for International Competitive Bidding (ICB) will follow Bank Group
Procurement Guidelines. Procedures for Local Competitive Bidding (LCB) shall be reviewed
by IDA and include public bid opening, clear evaluation criteria, local advertising and the
award shall be made to the lowest evaluated responsive bidder. Foreign bidders will not be
precluded from participating.
4.16   Procurement under the project would be undertaken as follows:
(a) Civil works
(i)      Civil works contracts at the sites in Kisumu and Mombasa (US$2.0
million) include the basic infrastructure consisting of access roads and
footpaths, water and drainage, electrical power installation, site
fencing and the construction of communal sanitary facilities,
construction materials store and site office. All contracts would be
awarded on the basis of LCB procedures among pre-qualified
construction firms in accordance with IDA Procurement Guidelines
and where foreign firms would not be precluded from participating.
(ii)    IDA would finance the construction of some of the individual
structures for each of the Jua Kali operators. Foreign imported
building materials (US$1.0 million) to be purchased under ICB
procedures (see para i below) would be procured by MRTT&T and
distributed to each of the Jua Kali locations. Jua Kali operators would
be responsible for the construction of individual structures using the
materials to be supplied, however, the type and method of
construction would be controlled and supervised and appropriate
training in building techniques given to operators, through the project,
to ensure a permanent and effective result.



40
(b) Q2gLs required for the project consisting of equipment, vehicles and construction
materials would be acquired as follows:
(i)      Equipment and vehicles (US$0.4 million) would be procured through
ICB following IDA Guidelines. Equipment (such as desk-top
computers and software) which have to be compatible with existing
units, as well as miscellaneous supplies which cannot be grouped into
bid packages of US$50,000 or more, may be procured through
"prudent shopping' by comparing prices obtained from at least three
independent suppliers. Items procured through "prudent shopping"
would be subject to an aggregate limit of US$0.3 equivalent.
Procurement of small quantities of low value equipment (computer,
software, etc.) may be purchased through IAPSCO, UNDP.
(c) Services
(i)      Technical assistance (including both long- and short-term
assignments) for training, studies and research will be provided by
firms and individual consultants. Selection procedures for all
consultants (firms and individuals) will be in accordance with the
Guidelines for the Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and
the World Bank as Executing Agency (August 1)81). Consultant
contracts estimated to cost less than US$100,000 do not require prior
Bank approval. All consultant terms of reference, qualifications,
experience and conditions of employment will be satisfactory to IDA
and subject to review Mforo an agreement (or contract) is negotiated
and signed. Procedur :s in consultancy contracts involving firms will
include the customa- mandatory review by IDA of proposed short
list, letter of invita-. In and award proposal for all contracts - in
particular evaluation criteria. Any proposals for single-source
selection of a consultant will be critically examined or reviewed by the
Bank and GOK.
(ii)    Overseas and In-country Training. For overseas training financed
under the credit, the qualifications of candidates, along with the course
of study, proposed training institutions, and costs, will be subject to
prior review by IDA. Given the importance of skills development and
training, the credit would support part of the local costs for local
training, which courses (along with their estimated costs), will also be
subject to prior IDA review.
(d) Operatine Expenses. Operating costs include local staff salaries, allowances,
office running expenses and fuel such as maintenance of vehicles, equipment,
office rent, supplies and utilites, for which procurement is not applicable.
Remaining expenses such as repairs, stationery, etc, would be financed by
Government using their own standard and established procedures.

4.17 Contract Review. During project implementation, IDA financed civil works contracts
above a threshold of TJS$250000; goods and equipment contracts above US$100,000; and, all
service contracts would bi subject to IDA's prior review procedures. Contracts below the
limits for civil works and gocds will be subject to post-award review. The review process
would cover 100 percent of total works contracts procured by ICB and about 14 percent of
the total value of Bank-financed goods and services. Sclected post-review of awarded
contracts below the threshold levels would be carried out on about 1 in 4 works contracts and
1 in 8 goods contracts. Terms of reference, curriculum vitae, draft contracts and letters of
appointment for all consultants would be reviewed by IDA prior to their appointment. The
Borrower should use, in all cases of IDA financed procurement, the Bank's Standard Bidding
Documents. As a condidon of Board presentation the GOK would provide draft tender
documents. including comnonent profile and terms of reference for implementation contracts
and a detailed procurement schedule (see para. 6.4(c)).
4.18   Recordin of Procurment Information. Procurement information will be collected
and recorded as follows:
(a) prompt reporting of contract award information by the Borrower;
(b) comprehensive quarterly reports to IDA by the Borrower (assisted by consultants
as necessary), indicating:
(i) revised cost estimates for individual contracts and the total project, including
best estimates of allowaxes for physical and price contingencies;
(ii) revised dming of procrement actions, including advertising, bidding,
contract award, and completion time for individual corracts;
(iii) compliance with aggregate limits on specified methods of procurement;
(e) a completion report by the Borrower within three months of the credit closing
date;
(d) at the time of supervision missions, a detailed statement of all procurement
undertaken to-date together with a forward forecast of anticipated procurement to
be undertaken in the coming year with estimate costs of disbursement.
E. Accounts and Audits
4.19 Other Accouints. FAD would maintain separate detailed accounts of all project
expenditures incurred through the project and by any other agencies involved in its activities.
FAD will ensure that all accounts are professionally mainained, that financial statements
suitable for auditing are drawn up, and that the project is audited anmnally by independent
auditors acceptable to IDA.

F. Project Implemeion
4.20   Status of Project Preparation. Government has prepared the following documents:
(a) a draft letter of informal sector policy and time-bound action plan; (b) a detailed project
implementation plan and procedures manual, including specific organizational arrangements,
actions (activities and procedures), responsible agents and annual budget estimates, at least for
the first year's implementation, within an attendant time schedule; (c) a terms of reference and
an advertisement for the recruittrent of an external auditor of the Training Levy Fund for
fiscal year 1993; (d) draft terms of reference for prospective implementation contracts; (e)
draft terms of reference for industrial training levy and trade testing assessments and policy
review process for skills training; and (f) evidence of (i) bonafide membership (registration
list) in the Jua Kali Association; and (ii) clear individual or Association ownership (copies of
letter of allotment purchase receipt, and leasehold title) of sites (Kisumu and Mombasa)
designated for infrastructure provision under the pro'ect.
4.21   Executina Agents. A government-private se4;tor partnership between the MRTT&T
and the Jua Kali Federation/Associations would comprise the chief executing agents for the
project. In the early years of the project, while the staff and management capacity of these
national institutions is being developed through a comprehensive staff development program,
the main project activities (training fund management, infrastructure and technological
information program development, policy analysis, and program monitoring and evaluation)
would be implemented with assistance from private sector consultant contract services (see
Annex 14-summary of principal contract areas and cost estimates). As a condition prior to
ne2otiation the GOK provided Draft Terms of Reference for prospective implementation
contracts (para. 6.2(d)). As a condition of credit effectiveness there wculd be full staffng of
the relevant MRTT&T jmplement  units required to assist in contract management (para.
6.5(c)).
4.22   The specific project coordinating and management functions would be as follows:
Day to day project coordination would be the responsibility of the Directorate of Applied
Technology (DAT), MRTT&T under the direct management of the Permanent Secretary,
MRTT&T (the chief accounting officer of the Ministry) and guided by a multi-sector (public
and private) Project Steering Committee (PSC) chaired by the Permanent Secretary with
representation from the Jua Kali Federation/Associations. The GOK would provide the
proposed staffing plan for staff to be redeployed and trained to assist in contract/project
management within the relevant units of the MRTT&T and the Jua Kali
Federation/lsociations.
4.23   The Training Fund would be managed by the Traming and Gender Unit (TGU) of the
Directorate of Applied Technology (DAT), MRTT&T. This unit would coordinate the
voucher program brokering (match-making between trainees and skdll-upgrading providers)
and sub-project appraisal of the contract training proposals from the public and private sector
providers with the assistance of training contract support services and the local Jua Kali
Associations. The Technology sub-component would also be implemented on a contract basis
and managed by the Center for Research and Technolog (CRT) and the Directorate of
Research Development (DRD). The pilot infrastructure component would be implemented by
engineering and management contract consultants under the management of the Applied
Technology Unit (ATU-infrastructure and worksite).



44
4.24 The Planning and Development Unit (PDU), MRTT&T would oversee contract
implementation of the staff development (capacity building of the Ministry and
Federation/Association) programs, manage the contractors undertaking the various policy
studies (assessment of the Training Levy Scheme and National Trade Testing Program) and
National Training Strategy development, performance monitoring of policy and program
implementation, and the impact evaluation. This activity would include the development of a
data base on the informal sector coordinating with overlapping data bases from the National
Economic Survey and the Household Monitoring Program. This would require the
coordination with the Central Bureau of Statistics (CBS). At the grassroots enterprise level,
the project activities would be implemented directly by the Jua Kali Federation/Associations
with support from the relevant MRTT&T units (described above) and related private sector
consultant contract services. It is envisaged that the Federation/Associations would be capable
of implementing and sustaining the training, technology and infrastructure programs upon
completion of the project. See Annex 3 for implementation organizational Charts of the key
executing bodies, MRTT&T and the Jua Kali Federation/Associations (national and district
levels).
4.25  lmplementation Schedule. The project will be implemented over the seven-year
period July 1, 1994 to June 30, 2001 with a Credit closing date of December 31, 2001. The
project implementation schedule (Annex 16), as well as the procurement and disbursement
schedules (Table 4.4 and Annex 13) would provide the basis for planning the physical aspects
of project implementation. During the joint annual review of project implementation (para.
4.28), Govermnent and IDA would review and update the implementation schedule as
necessary. A detailed program of activities and actions for the first year of implementation
would be developed by the relevant units described above prior to effectiveness to ensure
minimum delays to start-up activities. As a condition prior to negotiation the GOK provided
a detailed Draft Project Implementation Plan and Procedures Manual. including specific
organizational arrangements, actions (activities and procedures). responsible agents and annual
budget estimates. at least for the first year's implementation. within an attendant time schedule
(para. 6.2(b)). As a condition of Board presentation the GOK would provide an agreed
detailed Implementation Plan and a Procedures Manual. including specified organizational
arrangements. actions (procedures). and responsible agents within an agreed time schedule
(para. 6.4(b)).
4.26   Monitoring. Reporting and Evaluation. Overall monitoring and evaluation of project
performance will be carried out by the PDU over the life of the project, with the other units
playing a coordinating role. Implementation monitoring will assess the financial and physical
progress of the various components, as well as the progress in implementing the policy
measures supported by the project, with the most meaningful and practical indicators selected
for each component (Annex 17 and 19). The PDU will prepare draft formats monitoring
implementation on an annual basis, and finalize these after close consultation with other
implementing agencies and IDA. Monitoring instruments will be introduced at a project
launch workshop, organized by the PDU at the outset of the project, and participated in by all
agencies involved in the project. Utilizing these instruments, implementing agencies will
provide data to the PDU for inclusion in the consolidated semestrial progress reports. Such
reports would then be used in the preparation of a comprehensive annual progress report and
annual work plan for the following years activities. Government would prepare annual
reports summarizing achievements over the past year and proposed work programs for the



45
forthcoming year for all project components. Annual work programs would also include a
review of performance of project implementation personnel who would be hired on the basis
of one-year contracts. In addition, semi-annual progress reports will also be prepared by the
Borrower for review by IDA.
4.27   Progress Reporting. Detailed information on the status of project performance will be
maintained by the PDU, and made available to Bank supervision missions. In addition, semi-
annual progress reports will be prepared by the PDU and submitted to the Bank for
information and comment. MRTT&T will prepare an Implementation Completion report
(ICR) to be submitted to IDA not later than six months after the closing of the credit. During
negotiations. assurances were given by the Government that the MR1T&T would prepare:
semi-annual progress reports and forward them to the Bank by April 30 and October 31 of
each year (para. 6.3(d)) and an Implementation Completion Report (aCR) on the
imnementation experience and project outcomes for submission to IDA not later than six
months after the closing date (para. 6.3(e)).
4.28   Reviews. To provide a structure for regular reviews of project performance and
expenditures, MRTT&T would organize no later than May 31 of each year (to coordinate
with Government's budget preparation cycle), a joint Bank-Government review of project
implementation, based on its regular semi-annual progress reports (para. 4.27) and an annual
work program for the next fiscal year. During negotiations, assurances were given by the
Government that. beginninge in 1994, it would submit to the Bank for review and comment.
no later than April 30 of each year, a statement of project achievements and actual
e-xpenditures for the ongoing year and a work plan and proposed expenditures for the coming
3= (para. 6.3(f)). Reviews would include comments on: (a) major policies in the sector and
recurrent financing levels; (b) assessment of performance on the various project components;
(c) required modifications to targets and strategies; (d) the preparation of future work plans
and budgets; and, (e) statements received from MRTT&T and the other agencies responsible
for implementing the various project components. In view of the many legal actions due in
mid/late 1994 under the Action Plan, special prominence to review their status would be given
in each annual project review.
4.29   Mid-tenn Review. A mid-term review of the project, in addition to the planned
annual reviews proposed in paras. 4.27 and 4.28 above, will be carried out jointly by the
Government, Jua Kali Federation and the Bank no later than May 31, 1998. This review
will: (a) evaluate overall progress and project implementation relative to program and project
objectives; (b) identify constraints and bottlenecks by reviewing the adequacy of policies and
regulatory framework; (c) assess progress and suggest possible changes in training programs;
(d) assess progress in the completion and occupancy of the infrastructure works to be
constructed at the Jua Kali sites; (e) assess the success or failure to the provision of
satisfactory and protected land title to Jua Kali operators; and, (f) analyze institutional
constraints and make recommendations for change. During negotiation, assurances were
Fiven by the Government that MRTT&T would carry out with IDA and the Jua Kali
Federation. a tripartite Mid-Term review (based upon aereed policy and program performance
indicators) in 1998 (para. 6.3(g)).

V. BENEFITS AND RISKS
A. Benefits
5.1    Expected benefits of the project are: (a) improved skills and an expanded informal
sector - including the expansion of female-owned enterprises in the manufacturing sector --
with increased employment opportunities and earnings through improved product quality and
diversity, secure worksite and improved infrastructure, better marketing and increased access
to technology choice; (b) a downsized and more focused Ministry of Research, Technical
Training, and Technology capable of policy analysis, setting training standards, facilitating
technical and managerial training and related technology information and innovation; (c) the
progressive empowerment of the Jua Kali sector which will enhance its participation in the
economic development process; and (d) gradual inclusion (graduation) of the informal sector
into the modern sector by establishing a legal and contractual basis for its unfettered
development. Overall, project interventions (skill upgrading, technology information,
infrastructure provision and staff development) would target about 10% female participants
(proportional representation of women by region and trade). Whereas up to 50% basic skill
training under the voucher program would target women, given that they comprise only 10%
of the manufacturing sector.
5.2   To some extent the project's institutional benefits have already been achieved. During
preparation, a detailed analysis of the vocational and technical training system (VET) was
completed. The policies and actions proposed under this project will foster the reorientation
of the VET system to make it more efficient and market oriented. The project would help
develop and implement a national training strategy to improve the quality, access, and
sustainable financing of the VET system. Sub-projects would orient training institutions to
offer courses that are both market- and skill driven, such as skill upgrading and related
technology courses for the informal sector. Existing training capacity will be utilized and
improved, especially for skills upgrading. In the long term, the project would strengthen the
key planning functions of the MRTT&T. Establishing a monitoring and evaluation capacity at
MRTT&T, along with private sector association in the selection and operation of training
activities, will establish more automatic regulation of the VET system and make it more
responsive to the needs of the economy.
5.3    Table 5.1 presents a beneficiary summary. It is expected that some 60,000 Jua Kali
entrepreneurs and operators, primarily engaged in manufacturing, would benefit from the
proposed Training Fund. They represent approximately 50 percent of the manufacturing and
productive services in the informal sector, or 22 percent of entire informal sector. The
project would target enterprises consisting of 1-49 employees, located mainly in the urban and
peri-urban areas of Nairobi, Kisumu and Mombasa. About 4,000 individuals would benefit
through employment at the areas of infrastructure development; and close to 80,000 Jua Kali
Association members would benefit from information dissemination regarding appropriate
technology and skill upgrading programs.



47
