B. Risks
5.4   The possible risks, especially for the training and technology components, are: (a) the
inability of the Government to specify and implement training and private sector development
policies to enable informal sector entrepreneurs to operate more effectively; (b) poor
administration and over-sight of MRTT&T and Jua Kali Federation/Associations, including
poor decision-making about Training Fund allocation and management; (c) failure to recover
user costs for training, technology exchange visits and infrastructure development; and (d)
lack of capacity to sustain policy and project interventions. To overcome these risks, the
project would be conditioned on a policy letter and time-bound action plan ensuring key
enabling policies for the Jua Kali sector and the implementation of a National Training
Strategy. Training Fund replenishments would be allocated on an annual basis, once agreed
and acceptable levels of performance in administration, award allocation, and utilization
criteria are met. The process for awarding skill upgrading voucbers on a competitive basis.
training contracts and applied technology study tours and research grants would be evaluated



48
in an annual project review. A mid-term project evaluation would be included to allow for
project redirection, if necessary.
5.5    Since institutional development is a major component focusing on the Jua Kali
Associations and the MRTT&T, as well as management and planning skills of entrepreneurs,
one significant outcome would be the capacity to sustain policy and project interventions.
With the inclusion of partial cost recovery through training fees and a reformed Training
Levy, the financial sustainability of training investments would also be strengtiened. In
addition, cost recovery measures through the infrastructure revolving fund managed by the
Associations should contribute to sustainability of physical investments.
5.6    The infrastructure component will add several other risks: (a) coordination difficulties
with the various authorities responsible for land, planning and services; although land is
already acquired from local authorities, the process of surveying, mapping, and registration
may take a long time; (b) a high rate of default in terms of cost recovery; (c) uncertainty in
the upkeep and maintenance of the sheds and other infrastructure; (d) the sheds built under the
project may be sold or transferred to richer non-Jua Kalis; and (f) the self-help approach may
delay the construction of the infrastructure due to increased opportunity cost of timne devoted
by Jua Kali entrepreneurs. To mitigate against these possible risks, a detailed business plan
of Jua Kali Federation and Associations ensuring the maintenance of the infrastructure will be
prepared. Transparent and accountable procedures will be established for the allotment of
worksites. Finally, the project will also specify the stipulation disproving the transfer of
worksites to non-Jua Kalis.

VI. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
A. Agreements Reached During Negotiations
6.1    Government will open at the beginning of the project implementation period a special
account maintained by the Central Bank to facilitate ready availability of IDA funding, where

Accounting Department (FAD) at MRTT&T. Replenishment would be on the basis of actual
payments made and SOEs prepared as submitted by the accounts controller of MRIT&T
(FAD).

fulfilled prior to invitation to negotiate.

Plan (para. 3.3).
(b) A Detailed Draft Project Implementation Plan and Procedures'
Manual, including specific organizational arrangements, actions
(activities and procedures), responsible agents and annual budget
estimates, at least for the first year's implementation, within an
attendant time schedule (para. 4.25).
(c) Terms of reference for the recruitment of external auditors to audit the FY 93
Training Levy Funds (para. 3.5).
(d) Draft Terms of Reference for prospective implementation contracts
(para. 4.21).
(e) Draft Terms of Reference for trade test and industrial levy studies
and policy review process for skills training (para. 3.35).
(f)  Evidence of (i) bonafide membership (registration list) in the Jua Kali
Association; (ii) clear individual or Association ownership (copies of letter of
allotment purchase receipt, and leasehold title) of sites (Kisumu and Mombasa)
designated for infrastructure provision under the project (para. 3.26).
6.3    Assurances were provided during Negotiations. The Government provided the
following assurances at negotiations that:
(a) an assessment of the National Industrial Training Levy system and the trade
testing and certification system would be undertaken aad a detailed Action Plan
for modernizing these, including amendment of relevant acts, if necessary, would
be drafted by August 30, 1995. A policy review process for skills training and a



50
draft National Training Strategy would be completed by December 31, 1995
(para. 3.5);
(b) an organizational review of MRTT&T and Jua Kali Federation would be
completed by December 31, 1994. A suibsequent staff redeployment and
development plan required for successful project implementation would be
submitted to IDA by June 30, 1995 (para. 3.31);
(c) annual audits of special account and SOEs as well as of two financial accounts
for the infrastructure component by independent auditors acceptable to IDA
would be carried out. All audited accounts would be made available to IDA
within six months of the close of each fiscal year (paras. 3.28 and 4.13);
(d) MRTT&T would prepare semi-annual progress reports and forward them to the
Bank by April, 30 and October 31 of each year (para. 4.27);
(e) MRTT&T would prepare an Implementation Completion Report (ICR) on the
implementation experience and project outcomes for submission to IDA not later
than six months after the closing date (para. 4.27);
(f) beginning in 1994, MRTT&T would submit to the Bank, for review and
comment, no later than April 30 of each year, a statement of project

proposed expenditure for the coming year (para. 4.28);
(g) MRTT&T would carry out with IDA and the Jua Kali Federation, a tripartite
Mid-Term review (based upon agreed policy and program performance
indicators) in 1998 (para. 4.29).

D. Conditions of Credit Effectvene

(a) Employnment of external auditors to audit the FY 93 Training Levy Funds and

E. Conditions of Disburement
6.6   Conditions of Disbursement
(a) As a condition of disbursement on the civil works and building materials
categories of the infrastructure component, the GOK would submit the following
draft bills to Parliament, according to the Letter of Informal Sector Policy and
Action Plan: the Registration of Business Names Act, the Trade Licensing Act,
the Local Government Adoptive By-Laws (building standards), and the Electric
Power Act, as well as the Vagrancy, Nuisance, and Chief's Authority Acts
(para. 3.3).
6.7   Recommendation. With the above assurances, the proposed project forms a suitable
basis for an IDA Credit in the amount of SDR 15.7 million (US$ 21.8 million equivalent) to
the Republic of Kenya, at standard termis, with 40 years maturity.

REPUBLIC OF KENYA
MICRO- AND SMALL ENTERPRISE TRAINING AND TECHNOLOGY PROJECT
LETTER OF INFORMAL SECTOR POLICY AND ACTION PLAN
1.     The Kenyan economy faces a formidable employment challenge to resolve widespread
unemployr ent and underemployment as a result of slow economic growth and rapid population
growth. The estimated total labor force of 10.2 million in 1991 is expected to grow at an average
rate of about 4.1 percent between 1991 and 2000, which requires creating an average of 492,000
new jobs per year. If present trends continue, modem wage employment growing at 3.6% per
year will generate only 612,000 of the required jobs and in the small farm sector another 1.8
million by the year 2000. This leaves the balance of 2.5 million jobs to be created primarily in
me rural non-farm  and urban micro- and small enterprise (informal) sector, of which 75 percent
or 190,000 per year will be in the urban areas. A conservative estimate reports a total informal
sector employment of about 600,000 workers in 1991, 60 percent of which are in trade with the
remaining 40 percent halved by manuiacturing and services. Urban centers accounted for nearly
two thirds of total employment, while Nairobi makes up 25 percent of all employment in the
sector. 1/
2.     Over the last decade, the informal sector has made a substantial contribution to the
expansion of employment in Kenya. The informal sector constitutes 98 percent of all businesses
in the country and contributes 30 percent of total employment. Furthermore, three quarters of
jobs outside smallholding agriculture are created in this sector. Over the past two years, informal
sector employment grew at 14 percent per annum as compared to 2.3 percent in the modem wage
sector. The manufacturing sub-sector, in particular, showed higher employment growth (19%).
The informal sector manufacturing firms are an important support system for the modem sector
by providing domestic inputs used by medium and large-scale firms, besides being customers for
mnany goods produced in the modem sector. These two sectors are expected to have closer
linkages in the future through sub-contmcting etc.. While the low capital requirements and high
labor intensity of the informal sector makes it a promising area for absorbing employment, the
high turnover of one- and two- employee firms comprising the majority of informal sector
enterprises also make jobs precarious.
3.     While there is scarce information on the wages and income of the informal sector, the
few available statistics confirm the common perception that the informal sector workers comprise
the lower income group. For example, the 1991 estimates in Nairobi indicate Jua Kalis' average
monthly wage of Ksh 740 which is substantially below the minimum monthly wage of Ksh 964 in
Nairobi. A recently published Economic Survey, 1993 shows that an average income of informal
sector worker headed households is lower than formal sector worker's households: Ksh 3,420
versus Ksh 3,749 in Nairobi and Ksh 2,284 versus Ksh 3,498 in Eastern Province. Development
I/     The infonnal sector is an ambiguous term which describes a wide variety of types of smanl enterprises,
sometimes defined by 1--flhness or legality of activides, other times by number of employees, assets, amount of energy
use, etc. The proposed project adopts size of establishment measured by number of employees so that its beneficiaries can
be easily identified. Accordingly, the working definition is adopted to cover micro and small enterprises hiring less than
fifty employees. Further, the termn Jua Kali' is used colloquialy to refer to enterprises specializing in the manufacture of
products and providing productive services, rather than traders per so, who are the majority of informal sector
enftepreneurs. (See Para. 2.1, SAR for details.)



61
Annex 3
Page 2 of 7
of the informal sector, therefore, would contribute to poverty reduction by facilitating the active
participation of the Jua Kali in the economy.
IoLSorPoIicy
4.    In recent years, the Government of Kenya recognized the importance of the informal
sector in employment creation, income distribution, and rural-urban balance as spelled out in its
key sectoral policy papers. Sessional Paper No. I of 1986 on "Economic Management for
Renewed Growth' highlighted the informal and small-scale enterprises as a primary vehicle for
social and economic development. In line with the themes developed in this Sessional Paper, the
Sixth National Development Plan, 1989-93, acknowledged the neglect of the informal sector in
the past economic planning process and proposed the government's role as a catalyst and a
facilitator to promote the informal sector, leaving a major role for the private sector. Sessional
Paper No. 2 of 1992 on 'Small Enterprise and Jua Kali Development in Kenya" sets a
comprehensive policy framework which includes: (a) providing direct assistance for management
and entrepreneurial training and skill upgrading to individual entrepreneurs and small scale
enterprises; (b) promoting the transition of micro and small scale enterprises into medium size
enterprises; (c) expanding access to credit for the informal sector; (d) disseminating information
on market and appropriate technologies; and (e) providing enabling environment through
redressing licensing, tax, security of worksite and other regulatory restrictions on the informal
sector activities. The 1992 Sessional Paper foresees a major role for Ministry of Research,
Technical Training and Technology (MRTT&T) in the development and implementation of the
appropriate strategies to achieve these goals.
5.    The above mentioned policies have been implemented and the developed strategies have
been translated into actions if not to a full extent and at a slow pace. A database on institutions
involved in small-scale enterprise development activities has been established in order to give the
entrepreneurs the information for specific guidance ani assistance. Sheds (sites, services and
simple infrastructure) have been built for the small-scale and Jua Kali enterprises, and 60% of
ownership has already been transferred to the targeted beneficiaries. Moreover, MRTT&T
introduced entrepreneurship education in existing institutions and established Small Business
Centers (SBC) attached to the training institutions and Jua Kali Associations and other donor
sponsored projects to promote business skill acquisition and entrepreneurial behavior. The 1993
policy framework for MRTT&T foresees a more facilitating role than direct management of the
sector, including Jua Kali mobilization, skill upgrading, enabling environment, marketing and
publicity, applied technology and research and development services.
6.    The Government, while implementing the policies, realized that the development of the
informal sector would require the policy reform on the private sector as a whole, since the former
shares most of the constraints the latter faces. Accordingly, the recent bold steps taken by the
Government to promote the private sector development --ongoing parastatal reform, relaxation of
price control, elimination of import licensing in most areas and deregulation of interest rates--
would contribute to the provision of a better business environment for both formal and informal
enterprises. It is, however, admitted that the informal sector still suffers from a variety of
obstacles such as insecurity of worksites, limited access to formal credit, low skill levels, and
inability to acquire information on marketing and technology opportunities. In order to develop
the sector's full potential for employment creation and income generation more rapidly and in a
sustainable manner, a comprehensive strategy will encourage: (a) less State interference in private
sector decision making; (b) new boundaries between public and private sectors, e.g. no



62                           Annex 3
Page 3 of 7
government interference in private sector decisions on labor redundancies and wage guidelines; (c)
more labor market mobility; (d) export competitiveness; and (e) a shift of subsidies to wherever
we most clearly observe market failure due to poverty, e.g. vouchers. The Government intends
to pursue this strategy by reforming policies such as training, the legal and regulatory framework
and finance. This would be done on the basis of greater private sector involvement and cost
sharing.
7.    At the present time, the training system needs to be modernized for several reasons. It is
not flexible enough to prepare the workforce, including micro- and small enterprises, with the
technical skill upgrading required for new jobs and technologies as the structure of the economy
changes. Although there is some excess training capacity, there -re many unsustainable technical
training institutes that perpetuate low grade training, especially that provided by the Youth
Polytechnics (YPs). The Industrial Training Centers (ITCs) are only oriented to the large and
medium scale industries, although they now offer evening courses for Jua Kali artisans. The
trade test system does not adequately reflect new or informal skills and technologies, that are
found in the informal sector.
8.    As a whole, the current training system is ineffective and financially unsustainable in that:
(a) training provisions are not matched to demand, (b) consumers have little or no choice in the
type of training they want and need, (c) there is little evidence that technical/vocational training
creates jobs or generates income, and (d) private sector participation is low. There is a clear need
for more relevant employer-based training which would also facilitate moving skill upgrading
closer to productivity and employment. While the Industrial Training Levy is an important
private source of financing training in the various industries, at present the levy system needs
modernization in order to have an impact on labor productivity.
Steps Toward a National Traiinng Stratea
9.    To address the above issues, the Government, through the MRTT&T, intends to develop a
long term National Training Strategy. It would be more responsive to productive sector training
needs and based on (i) an assessment of the Industrial Training Levy scheme, (ii) an evaluation of
the trade test and certification system, and (iii) a policy review process for skills training. These
would result in a draft National Training Strategy elaborating, among others, future objectives,
organization and management, as well as financing and content (institutions, curricula and
certification) of the future training systems.
10.   Further development and implementation of a National Training Strategy would be an
outcome of the Institutional Development Component of the project under the Policy Analysis,
Monitoring and Evaluation sub-component. Both policy analysis capacity and the implementation
of the Project Training Fund would facilitate project sustainability and long term sectoral
development. For instance, the Project Training Fund design provides a model of a demand
driven skill training system embodying the principles proposed for the National Training Strategy:
(a) relevance (demand-driven), (b) quality and efficiency, and (c) financial sustainability (i.e.,
budget allocation will be competitive and gradually shift from subsidy to cost sharing) allowing
training institutions more autonomy for financing.



63
Annex 3
Page 4 of 7
11.   The Project Training Fund would develop financing mechanisms, i.e., a Jua Kali training
fund as well as experience and lessons to be incorporated in the modernization of the Industrial
Training Levy scheme and thus enable project sustainability. This process is likely to involve
consolidation and privatization of training, facilitating a larger role for the private sector and
enterprises in skills training, especially regarding training facilities and cost sharing, and
improving mechanisms linking the Government, industry and workers.
Informal Sector Enablinn Environment
12.   The informal sector faces various institutional obstacles to entry, exit, and operation --
including growth and contraction of an enterprise. Some of these obstacles are shared by the
private sector as a whole but tend to affect the informal sector more seriously, while others affect
the informal sector only. To be more specific, while the informal sector suffers from the general
constraints such as lack of macroeconomic discipline, an uneven enforcement of laws and
excessive regulations, and poor infrastructure, it is further constrained by lack of worksite
security and basic infrastructure and limited access to formal credit. These factors are again
mutually reinforcing.
13.   Enterprises in Kenya are required both to register and obtain the various licenses. These
requirements may inhibit starting and operating informal sector enterprises due to their
complicated process and high monetary and transaction costs. The registration process ipvolves
two stages: first, company registration with the Registrar of Companies in the Office of the
Attorney General, and second, a series of mandatory national registration with various line
ministries. Without registration, licenses cannot be obtained thereby blocking access to formal
credit. Under the Registration of Business Names Act, company registration is automatic but may
be cumbersome for informal sector entrepreneurs since it can only be carried out in Nairobi.
While the registration fee is trivial for small-scale enterprises, it can cost up to four percent share
value to larger limited liability companies, therefore, being a detriment to the growth of
enterprises in the long run. The second stage requires five separate registrations from four
ministries: manufactures registration and Bureau of Standards (Ministry of Commerce and
Industry), National Social Security Fund and Occupational Health (Ministry of Labor and
Manpower Development), the Central Bureau of Statistics (Ministry of Planning and National
Development) and local authorities (Ministry of Local Government). While registration
applications are rarely denied, the procedures are complicated and frequent delays are reported,
leading to large transaction costs for quick completion. The Management Information System
(MIS) has been recently introduced in the Attorney General's Office to expedite the process, but
the procedure would be decentralized to be able to register outside Nairobi and be further
combined and simplified.
14.   There are three main classes of licenses affecting the informal sector: national licenses
required under the Trade Licensing Act (TLA), those under other special Acts, e.g. health and
tourism, etc. and the licenses issued by local authorities. National licenses are isstied according
to the type of business activity: Trade License for manufacturing, General License for
wholesaling, a Retailers License for retail distribution, etc.. Licenses are also issued for specific
localities and product/service lines and any changes in these characteristics require official
revision. Each license is valid only for one year and its renewal requires the same procedures as
starting new firms. The license fees vary by activity and location but are not graduated by the
size of the firm, discriminating against small-scale firms. In 1992, Trade License fees were Ksh
6,600 in Nairobi and Ksh 3,300 elsewhere. These were too high to be paid annually by the



64
Annex 3
Page 5 of 7
informnal sector operators. In addition to national licenses, there are a range of licenses required
by the local authorities. There is no statutory requirement for harmonization across local
authorities in the number of licenses or the level of fees. Since local licenses are issued in a
manner similar to national licenses, the adverse effect of local licenses is more serious on the
informal than the formal sector for the same reasons. In principle, obtaining a license should not
be difficult nor too expensive, but, in practice, it is cumbersome and costly: there are no explicit
criteria for refusing to grant a license and no formal mechanism for redress, leaving wide scope
for harassment and rent seeking in the licensing process. The Trade Licensing Act would be
amended to streamline the licensing processes through consolidation of licenses, establishment of
a "one stop service" for acquisition of licenses and elimination of the current annual renewal
requirement.
15.   Another major institutional barrier to entry into the informal sector is found to be the lack
of access to land for enterprises, in particular access to a title deed on the land. Absence of lIngal
titles contributes to lack of access to credit and to ha .ment by local authorities, and provides
disincentives for development of and investment in t..  -orksite preventing improvements in
technology and productivity. In order to solve this problem, the Government will ensure that
more public land is allocated to Jua Kalis in order to facilitate their access to urban land and thus
create security of worksites.
16.   In addition to these constraints to entry and growth of the informal sector shared by the
informal sector, there are various other legal, regulatory barriers which specifically affect
operation of the informal sector only. For instance, the Vagrancy Act. Chief Authority and
Public Nuisance Acts are outdated laws enacted in the colonial times to control the Africans.
These laws, however, justify local authorities' harassment for the informal sector and therefore
hamper the growth of the sector. There are a range of laws regulating building standards such as
Local Government Adoptive By-Laws (Building) Order. and Public Health Act. While these laws
serve different purposes, they set up building standards unrealistic for Jua Kalis who are thereby
subject to harassment. Additional outdated or inappropriate laws which impede the sector are the
Electric Power Act. Shop Hours Act. Mombasa Shop Hours Act, etc.. To promote the sector
development, the Government undertakes to review and amend any laws found to be inhibiting the
operation of the informal and the private sector in general. In this regard, the Govermnent has
already formed several Task Forces to review outdated laws that were enacted in the colonial
times, with a view to having them either repealed or amended accordingly as the case may be.
17.   The laws to be reviewed include those relating to building standards such as the Local
Govermnent Adoptive By-Laws (Building) Order among others. In the case of building standards,
it has been realized that while these laws serve different purposes, they set up unrealistic
standards for most Kenyans. They have also been overtaken by recent research on low cost
building materials.
EFance
18.   Lack of access to credit is a major obstacle to the growth of the informal sector. The
Government, however, recognizes that an increased flow of funds to small scale enterprises will
only be achieved if financing is carried out on a self-sustainable basis, at commercial rates of
interest, mainly through domestic funds mobilization and without subsidies either to the
beneficiaries or the providers of such financing. In this regard, the Government will continue to
pursue the financing of the sector under these principles. The Government will also encourage
the establishment of financial institutions specialized in the financing of the informal sector
enterprises.

REPUBLIC, OF KENYA
MICRO- AND SMALL ENTERPRISE TRAINING AND TECHNOLOGY PROJE CT
THE KENYA NATIONAL JUA KALI FEDERATION
Background
1.    The Kenya National Jua Kali Federation is an umbrella organization of Jua Kali
associations in Kenya, estimated at more than 400. Each association has approximately 100
to 500 members. The Federation was founded in February 1992 in accordance with the Laws
of Kenya for small enterprises as outlined in the Small Enterprise Strategy Sessional Paper of
May, 1989. One of its main goals is to involve Jua Kali artisans directly in Jua Kali
development activities and to work with the various private sector organizations and
government institutions to ensure that the needs of the Jua Kali artisans are provided for. The
Government assumes a facilitating role.
2.    The associations started forming as far back as 1986. When the Ministry of Technical
Training and Applied Technology (the former MRTT&T) came into existence in 1988, it
encouraged Jua Kali artisans countrywide to forn associations, to make it easier to support
the sector. Many associations were registered with the Registrar General. The Federation, a
national body, was established as a private organization registered under the laws of Kenya to
represent individual Jua Kali artisans through their local associations.
3.    About 300 out of 380 associations countrywide participated in the elections that gave
birth to the Federation in 1992. Since its inception, the Friedrich Ebert Stiftung 1/ (FES)
has been funding the Federation's operations. This relationship originated from FES's long-
term support to the development of Jua Kali entrepreneurs in the form of promoting credit
guarantee schemes which aimed at introducing these entrepreneurs to formal credit and
banking systems. For the scheme to operate effectively and efficiently, it was imperative to
organize the operators/beneficiaries into units of self help organizations called the Credit
Guarantee Associations. This experience in organizing the Jua Kali entrepreneurs became
very valuable during the initial phases of organizing the Kenya National Federation of Jua
Kali Associations.
4.     At the formative stage of the Federation, the FES provided support and advise on how
to organize an effective apex body for Jua Kali operators. Firstly, in February 1992, FES
agreed to fund the first delegates' conference which inaugurated the Federation. The first
national chairman and 24 officials (three from each province) were elected on February 26,
1992. The elections were held under the supervision of the MRTT&T. Secondly, in May
1992, FES funded the first Board of Directors management workshop at the Government
Training Institute (GTI) in Mombasa which provided useful insights into proper management
approaches including drawing up long term planning strategies for future growth of the
Federation. 2/
I/    Foundation.
2/     National Federatiou of Jua Kali Associations. Lone Termn Strategic Planning Workshop. GTI: Mombasa
25th to 28th May 1992. Draft Report prepared by T. Ochien'g Oyieke.



70
ANNEX 5
Page 2 of 6
Aims and Objectives - Short and Long Term Strategy Plan 31
5.     The establishment of the Kenya National Federation of Jua Kali Associations was
aimed at creating an institution to represent the interests of Jua Kali operators which in tuM
would improve their business growth and provide a pressure group base.
More specifically, the Federation would:
- establish a dialogue with the Government about the needs, opportunities and
responsibilities of the Jua Kali sector;
- seek and ensure better recognition of the Jua Kali sector by the Government and
local authority, and assist the Government to create an enabling policy environment for Jua
Kalis;
- act as a primary vehicle for consultation, exchange of experience and coordination
between all ministries, financial institutions, donor agents, and NGOs;
- provide a consultative platform where the search for policy direction and programs
as well as review of the results relating to Jua Kali activities, can be shared;
- improve Jua Kali performance and economic development of the sector (i.e.,
increase the operators' income) by facilitating access to training, skill upgrading, production
technologies and infrastructure;
- improve market awareness by both Jua Kali artisans and their clients through local
and external trade exhibitions and fairs, show rooms for Jua Kali products, Jua Kali trade
magazine, catalogue of Jua Kali products for export, and air time on radio and TV;
- provide increased and better market outlets through strategies on marketing
channels, government tenders for local markets and sub-contracting with larger informal (and
formal) enterprises;
- improve access to finances by Jua Kali operators through issuance of title deeds on
Jua Kali plots to be used as collateral, establishing credit guarantee schemes, and pooling of
funds to create credit schemes in primary associations.
Constitution and Rules 4/
6.     The Federation as a private organization registered under the Laws of Kenya and
subject to statutory regulations. The elected office bearers of the Federation include the
National Chairman, the National Vice Chairman, the National Secretary, the National
Assistant Secretary, the National Treasurer, and 16 provincial representatives, 2 from each
province. They constitute the Board of Directors and are elected at the members
3/     Kenya National Pederation of Jua Kali Associatins. Short and Lom Tenn Strateev Plan. Nairobi.
Jnualy 1993.
neKThe Kenya National Federation of Jua Kali Associations. Constitution and Rules. Nairobi, June 1993.



71                          ANNEX 5
Page 3 of 6
representative meeting to be held every 5 years. All office bearers hold office from the date
of the election until the 5th succeeding annual members representative meeting. Elections
may be supervised by officers of the ministry in charge of the Jua Kali sector. The Board
meets twice a year and is chaired by the National Chairman.
7.    The Federation shall by resolution in the General Meeting appoint no less than 3 and
not more than 5 Trustees in whom shall be vested all its property under the direction of the
Board. The Trustees have authority to generally represent and act for the Federation in
matters of business transactions. Each Trustee shall apply for incorporation under the
Perpetual Succession Act in the name of "Registered Trustees of the Kenya National
Federation of Jua Kali Associations."
8.    The Federation has an Executive Committee consisting of: the Chairman, Vice
Chairman, the Secretary, the Treasurer, the Organizing Secretary, the Executive Director and
the Trustees of the Federation. The Committee meets once in every quarter.
Management of the Federation
9.    The Federation has a permanent Searetariat based at its headquarters in Nairobi and
other branches as may be deemed fit from time to time by the Board of Directors. The
Secretariat is headed by the executive officer who acts in an advisory capacity to the Board of
Directors. See Annex 4, Chart 2 for the administrative structure of the National Jua Kali
Federation and Jua Kali Associations.
10.   The Board of Directors in consultation with the Secretariat and the Trustees manages,
controls, and improves the entire assets, immovable and movable of the Federation and
collects and administers in its discretion the revenue of the Federation. The Board has the
power to borrow money upon such terms and conditions (and invest the funds) as it sees fit,
in consultation with the Trustees.
11.   The Board of Directors is empowered to by-laws for regulating the affairs of the
Federation and the Jua Kali sector, including the appointment of auditors. The books of
account and all documents relating thereto and a list of the members shall be availed for
inspection at the registered office of the Federation by any officer or member of the
Federation.
12.   The Board has absolute control and management of all Jua Kali exhibitions organized
by the Federation and full power over all premises belonging or leased by the Federadon.
The Board of Directors or the Executive Committee may perform their respective duties
through sub-committees.
Membership
13.   Any registered (at MRTT&T) Jua Kali association is eligible for membership of the
Federation on payment of an entrance fee and subject to the approval of the Board of
Directors.
14.   Every individual artisan pays a monthly subscription fee (at present Ksh 10), to be
determined from time to time by the Board of Directors, through his/her member association.



72
ANNEX 5
Page 4 of 6
15.   Any member association may be expelled (or may resign) from membership if the
Executive Committee and Board of Directors so recommend, i.e., if proven guilty of any
improper or dishonest conduct.
16.   In general, the Jua Kali artisan does not fall under the Workman's Compensation Act
nor is he/she an employee in the true sense of the word. Many Jua Kalis are self-employed
and work under conditions that are likely to cause harm to them and they will not be able to
recover the loss from anywhere. The question has been asked whether Jua Kali artisans
should be provided for under an independent Act of Parliament so that they are protected.
Sources of Revenue and Federation Sustainability
17.   At present, the Federation is being funded by the Friedrich Ebert Stiftung which
strongly supports the idea of the Federation. FES has committed itself to fund office space
and staff salaries (i.e., the Executive Officer, Secretary/Manager) and other operating
expenses until December 1993. The long-term goal is to become self-sustaining mainly
through: registration fees (which is Ksh 500 at present), the individual monthly contribution
fee (which is Ksh 10 at present), and services that the Federation will be giving. These
include profits from the Business Development Centres (BDC), selling materials, tools,
equipment and spare parts, interest from credit schemes, fee for using machinery at the BDC
and profit from the Jua Kali magazine, rental fee from the Federation premises.
18.   There are approximately between 100 and 500 members per association. It is
expected that members' contribution and subscriptions will keep on growing year after year.
This requires above all sustainability of the Federation and recruitment of members.
However, other factors will determine the extent to which the Federation will succeed in
recruitment, including: the leadership and the commitment of the Federation officials and the
secretariat staff as well as the Jua Kali Associations' officials; the political climate in the
country; and the tangible benefits (i.e., improved access to training and technology) for
artisans and enterprises.
19.   Most potential members will join the Federation when they can see the benefits of
doing so. However, the indirect benefits, although they affect almost everyone equally, seem
to be less of an incentive. These include Jua Kali policies and other laws that affect the Jua
Kali business growth. These general benefits are the most important to the sector as they
create an enabling environment. The direct benefits, such as access to training, infrastructure,
credit facilities, title deeds etc., are strong incentives to join the Federation. Therefore, the
role of Federation officials and association leaders is very crucial in explaining to the artisans
the benefits of having a Federation.
20.   In the absence of a viable extension network to diffuse new technologies, skills and
information directly to Jua Kali operators, the associations are a bonafide private sector
institutional apparatus to perform this function. The Federation is presently engaged in a
national recruitment drive to enlist existing associations and help form new ones.

Mombasa Jua Kali Association _/
21.   The Association has 840 paid up active members. It is registered with the Registrar
of Societies and therefore subject to statutory regulations. The Association Committee,
comprising a Chairman, Vice Chairman, Secretary, Treasurer and Assistant Treasurer, is
elected annually by the association members. Audited accounts are produced annually and
circulated to the members prior to the annual general meeting.
5/    Ihis case study is based on staiscs from the Mombasa Jua Kali Assocation tt_bhip foms and a
survey conducted by J. Stevens, Technical Coopeion Officer, Mmasa Polytechic (October, 1990).



ANNEX 5
Page 6 of 6
22.   The working conditions of these Jua Kali members are diverse and range from
established workshops with premises to operations carried out completely in the open with no
protection from the environment. Many artisans operate their businesses on the side of the
road.
23.   The present law is not clear on the question of whether or not Jua Kali artisans should
be taxed and if so, what are the modalities involved in such taxation, including the operation
of VAT. Jua Kali artisans are often illiterate and do not understand the constitutions either of
their associations or the Federation. Thus, they may not know the legal limits within which
they must operate.
24.   A sample of 224 paid up members of the Mombasa Jua Kali Association, about one
third of the total membership, was taken for analysis and the results are shown below:
- The 224 JK members employ 745 workers and 462 trainees which is an average of
5 people per JK member.
- The majority of JK members employ both workers and trainees:
- 175 of the members employ one or more workers
- 146 of the members have trainees
- 134 of the members employ workers and have trainees
- 46 of the members work alone
- Over 94 of the registered members operate without premises. In some cases a
workshop is given for a roadsiJe working area. The average workshop size for the sample is
approximately 3500 square feet. Without doubt a high percentage of those with premises
operate in restricted or unfavorable conditions.
- Bank accounts are held by most of the JK members, although very few have
bankloans. Partnerships are not a common feature accounting for about 20% of the
membehip.
- Only 29 members have received full time training in a training institution (less than
six months).
- The most dominant activity of the JK members is metal fabrication and welding.
Other activities comprise carpentry and woodwork, motor vehicle, blockmaking and building,
handeraft and dressmaking, general engineering, and electical wiring. Welding machines,
hand drills and hand tools are common, but there is a serious shortage of machine tools.

REPUBLIC OF KENYA
MICRO- AND SMALL ENTERPRISE TI4NING AND TECHNOLOGY PROJECT
STRUCTURE, CAPACITY AND FINANCING OF THE TRAINING (VET) SECTOR
Traning Institutions, Capacity and Progams
1.    Vocational Education and Training (VET) in Kenya is generally identified with the
formal training system. However, it is estimated that there are about 2,000 training
institutions of different types in the country. These include private training institutes with an
estimated annual enrollment of about 23,500 and approximately 100 Public Service Institutes
under the Directorate of Personnel Management (DPM) which facilitates skill upgrading for
about 11,000 civil servants.
2.    Basic statistics of formal training differ and should be taken as an approxination: the
number of institutions, their total enrollment and their annual intake and outputs. This is due
to a lack of technical capacity to monitor and evaluate a wide range of training institutions for
which MRTT&T, a relatively new ministry, has inherited responsibility.
3.    The formal technical and vocational training sector in Kenya comprises the following
levels: (1) artisan, 2 years; (2) craftsman, 3 years; (3) technician, 3 years; (4) technologist, 2
years. In principle, each level includes a compulsory period of industrial apprenticeship, i.e.,
6 months for artisan and technologist students and 9 months for craftsmen and technician
students. Five types of institutions offer training at the various levels: The Youth
Polytechnics (YP) and the National Youth Service (NYS) offer training at the artisan level.
The Industrial Training Centers (ITC), the Institutes of Technology (IT) and the Technical
Training Institutes (TTM) offer training at the craftsman level. The National Polytechnics (NP)
train technicians. The state universities train technologists and masters of technology.
Public Training Istitutions
Youth Polytechnics - YP (330)
Technical Training Institutes - TTI (18)
Institutes of Technology - IT (17)
Industial Training Centers - ITC (Nairobi, Kisumu, Mombasa)
Kenya Textile Training Institute - KTTI (Nairobi)
National Polytechnics - NP (Nairobi, Mombasa, Eldoret)
Kenya Technical Teachers Training College - KTTC (Nairobi)
Universities (of Nairobi, Kenyatta, Egerton, Jomo Kenyatta
University of Agriculture and Technology and Moi University)
4.    Thie main artisan or first level training institutions are the YPs whose main objective
is to equip primary school leavers with artisan skills for self or other employment. There are
about 590 YPs, 260 of which are community supported. In 1989, total enrollment was



76                                 ANNEX 6
Page 2 of 13
estimiated at about 40,000. Given the training duration of two years, this implies an annual
intake of about 20,000.
5.      Table 1 (page 10) shows enrollments and capacity in the various training institutions.
The actual capacity for the YPs is not known, but estimated at about 70 percent. In addition
to the YPs, there are 16 National Youth Service (NYS) training centers and the Christian
Industrial Training Centers (CITCs), which also provide artisan level training.1/
Underutilization is as follows: TTI: 28.8%  of existing facilities; IT: 32%; NP: 11.7%. Low
enrollment is mainly in higher diploma courses rather than in diploma courses at the NPs.
This is caused by a shortage of students who meet entry requirements coupled with
employers' unwillingness to sponsor higher diplon.as for their workers. In addition, more
flexibility in NPs appears to be required to meet stuc:-nts' demand for training, e.g., removal
or revision of unpopular courses such as welding and fabrication offered as diploma courses.
The full utilization of KTTC faci'ties accounts for demand for teaching positions in technical
training.
6.      The number of apprentices sponsored by irdustry has been declining to less than 50%
of operating capacity. The low enrollme!o. -i the ITCs is probably due to a decrease in the
number of apprentices caused by low indusiial economic activity. However, more needs to
be known about the incentives or disincentives for established industries to provide on-the-j .
training for employees and to take on apprentices/trainees. Currently, the ITCs enroll a
growing number of self-sponsored trainees. Evening skill upgrading courses are also
increasing.
7.      Another explanation for low enrollments may be inadequate recruitment practices.
The DIT is responsible for allocating apprentices to the three ITCs. At times students from
Mombasa may be sent to Nairobi. The policy of distributing the students around the country
requires each institution to have boarding facilities. The same holds true for ITs and TTIs,
wh', advertise nationwide for students for the various trades. All applicants are pooled
centrally and allocated to the institutions on the basis of an equal national distribution.
8.      All told, the number of people with technical, vocational and university training has
almost doubled over the past ten years. However, in 1992, the institutions providing artisan
and craft level training have an annual intaice of no more than 30,000, whereas the pool of
potential recruits averages around 250,000.
9.      Most VET institutions base their courses on the 8-4-4 curriculum. Under the 8-4-4
system introduced in 1985, emphasis is placed on vocational/technical education in the last
two years of primary school and throughout secondary school, and on integrating the large
network of vocational iuaining inqtitutions more closely into the formal system. It appears,
however, difficult to implement this new curriculum because there are not the required
/       Ihe NYS, which is under the Office of the Prcsident, recruits volunteers anmually to undertake large-scale projects,
such as food production, road construction, land clearance, and tree pntng, outside the scope of community self help efforts.
The Christian Industria Training Centres (CITC) of Kenya also provide industrial (engineering crafts) and commercial skills to
needy youths. All centers apart from Kisumu train artisans. The CITC in Kisumu offers secretarial training. CITCs provide
their trainees to serve the church and the nation through the produedon unit while at the center and after completion of the two
year course.



77
ANNEX 6
Page 3 of 13
teachers, technology, i.e., additional equipment, nor the funds to do so. Serious concerns
have been expressed by some members of the Kenyan education community about falling
academic standards, considered the tradeoff of a more technically oriented curriculum.
10.   The introduction of the 8-4-4 school system may be contributing to the low enrollment
in the YPs, TTIs and Tis. The system needs ,nstructors with technical qualifications many do
not have. Student fees are also higher. Student confidence in the programs has also declined.
TTIs, for instance, have changed from being technical and trade schools for post-primary
training into technical secondary schools offering a four-year program in academic technical
education and then back again to technical training institutes offering two and three years of
training to primary school leavers. With the introduction of the 8-4-4 system, they now admit
students who have completed forrn four level of (secondary) education.
11.   Changes in craft curriculum development have also affected the three Industrial
Training Centers (ITCs). Craft curriculum development was originally based on the City and
Guilds System, but from 1980 to 1986, with ILO assistance the Government introduced the
concept of modules of employable skills, called the Kenyan Integrated Training System
(KITS). After substantial investments (KSH 6,1 million) the system was abandoned. The
main problems seem to have been short-term vision and planning as well as lack of consensus
regarding course content.
Interna Efficiency
12.    Little is known about internal efficiency. There are no data on drop out and
repetition. The Kenya National Examinations Council undertakes examinations biannually.
Results of July, 1992, are as follows: (1) Very few students from only two YPs took the test.
(2) Only 43.2% of TTI students passed the test. 22.7% failed and 34.1% would take the test
again but do not have to repeat the course. (3) In the ITs the pass rate is 54%, failure rate
9% and referred state 36% (referred means no repetition of courses but of test to advance to
the next grade. (4) The Pass rate in the NPs is 51 %, failure rate 14%, referred state 35%.
13.   Staffing is generally inadequate. There are many vacant positions, common in the
public sector. On average, only 64% of teaching positions in the NPs have been filled. The
high vacancy rate seems to be related to low salaries. An average salary for teachers in NPs
was Ksh 6,259 per month in 1992, about US$ 96.
Training in the Informal (Jua Kali) Sector
14.   Apprenticeship training in this sector accounts for the largest number of trainees.
Sildls of a trade are acquired in the Jua Kali artisans' workshop 2!. Most Jua Kali business
owners nave acquired their own skills within the sector. On average they have more than two
trainees. Apprenticeship training is almost free of charge, although parents pay the
trainer/master a fee up to KSH 2,500. Some Jua Kali entrepreneurs provide their trainees a
small subsistence allowance. However, training in the informal sector often takes place
without any kind of guarantee that the trainee will receive even the minimum level of required
2      In the Jua Kali sector, food processing, metal fabrication, small scale construction businesses can be found, with I to
10 employees, as well as garment, textiles, crafts, and tailoring businesses on an even smaller scale, with I to S employees.

owners have acquired their own skills within the sector. On average they have more than two

trade skills. Some studies suggest that craft training in Jua Kali is frequently scanty,
incomplete and unsystematic, due to limited knowledge levels of the Jua Kali trainers
themselves as well as inadequate facilities and training resources. Qualified instructors (i.e.,
master craftsmen) are extremely limited.
15.   The Directorate of Applied Technology (DAT), which is in charge of Jua Kali
development programs in MRTT&T, has started information gathering and development
program for the Jua Kali sector. The artisans are asked to fill out a questionnaire, providing
basic statistical information on this little known sector, including their apprenticeship
background. After screening by a local Jua Kali association, artisans are registered with
MRTT&T. Artisans are also encouraged to register with the Registrar of Societies, as a
prerequisite for obtaining credit and/or a license to open a business.
16.   Since 1990, 10 Technical Training Institutions have established pilot Small Business
Centers (SBC) to promote entrepreneurship education. These centers also act as test and
workshop sites to conduct a variety of business activities within the local community. SBCs
are supposed to form a close network of facilities also serving nearby businesses and
promoting the development of small and Jua Kali enterprises through linkages between the
training institution and the business community. Services provided both to potential and
practicing entrepreneurs include training in business planning, technical assistance and
information services.
17.    The educational background of Jua Kali entrepreneurs3/ can be described by the
number of years they had education at the various levels (see Table A.). The following
observations can be made: the distribution of primary and secondary attendance is similar to
the national average; only 23.7% of all entrepreneurs received formal technical training in
comparison to 61.2% who had informal (technical) training; only 4.1 % of entrepreneurs
attended commercial training of about 1.3 years on the average.

National Trade Testing
18.   The national trade test system was established in 1963. It has grown in size (Table B)
and has become an important source of income for the DIT (190,000 Kenya Pounds in 1992/3
(Table C). Trade testing is the only certification of practical performance ability. However,
the effectiveness of the system has been declining, in part because of the sheer volume
handled and the lack of computerization.

19.   At present there are 23 apprenticeable trades. With the constant curriculum changes,
the amount of theory and practice in the various programs has also been changing, which has
affected the credibility of the trade test certificate as a reliable indicator of a worker's
practical ability. This undermines a professionally sound base for recruitment of artisans, as
well as their mnobility, in the Kenyan industry, formal and informal.
Financing of Formal Training
20.   The technical and vocational training institutions have a variety of financing sources.
Both development and recurrent budgets may be private (i.e., employers and community),
government and donor supported. The Youth Polytechnics and Institutes of Technology,
many of which owe their origin to community, religious and foreign donor initiatives,
continue to receive community and parental support. The Government provides instructors as
well as grant-in-aid based ori enrollment. Instructors may be on various payrolls such as
Public Service Commission (PSC), Teachers Service Commission (TSC), or direct
employment by the training institution.
21.   Table C illustrates the scope for cost sharing and cost recovery at various levels of
training, especially at the ITCs and associated trade testing.

22.   Most YPs are in a weak financial position due to insufficient funding. Cuts in
Government budgets in recent years and inefficiencies in the management of the institutions,
including poor collection of fees, have resulted in inadequate facilities and training materials,
and the deterioration of the quality and amount of training. Similarly, the ITs have not been
able to retain the previous level of support, which they apparently enjoyed und2r the
Harambee system. Failed income generating productions have also weakened the financial
base of the ITs, many of which are bankrupt and have large debts. Their poor financial
situation has led to ever increasing tuition fees, almost non-existent capital investments and
declining quality of training. In addition, the institutions suffer from inefficiencies, including
a high number of non-teaching staff. Many of these institutions can neither afford nor
maintain the technology and machinery required to update programs, especially since the
introduction of the more comprehensive curriculum under the 844 system.
23.   The education share in total central Government expenditure is estimated at 14.7%
(1991/92) and 12.8% (1992/93). The training (VET) share in total central Government
expenditure represents only about 2% of the education budget in 1992 and 2.4% in 1993
respectively. Of this share, the National Polytechnics and Institutes of Technology (IT)
receive most in contrast to the Youth Polytechnics, which are more likely to produce informal
sector operators. As a whole, the education sector has the largest share of Governnent
expenditure in social services, accounting for 76.7 percent and 59.1 percent of the recurrent
and development expenditures, respectively for 1992/93. Table D shows central Government
expenditures on formal education and training.

Per Student Costs of Training and Sources of inance/Cost Recovery
24.   Table 2 (page 11) shows estimated per student cost in technical training institutes and
the estimated Government cost as percentage of total costs. While the NPs are highly
subsidized, the ITCs receive no subsidy for student fees. Most trainees in the ITCs are
sponsored by their employers and do not pay fees. The Government is responsible for the
physical facilities of the ITCs, while the Industrial Levy Fund pays for the running costs.
25.   Table 3 (page 12) shows projected per student actual annual costs for technical
training and cost recovery. At present, training and boarding fees are under review, although
the political climate makes the proposed increases in fees socially sensitive. The proposed
increases are listed under combined Government and Private Cost per Student, while the
actual fees collected in the past are listed under Cost Recovery. Table 4 (page 13) indicates
the fees structure in technical training institutes.

The Indusrial Trainng Levy Fund
26.    The Industrial Training Levy 4/ is a private source of financing craft and technical
training through the Industrial Training Act, established in 1971. The Act makes provision
for the regulation of the training of persons engaged in industry with a view to improve the
quality and efficiency of manpower engaged in industry and to ensure an adequate supply of
properly trained manpower at all levels in industry.
27.    All employers are required by law to register with the DIT and to pay an industrial
training levy in accordance with their Industrial Training Levy order classifications set out in
the Act. The DIT collects levy payments from employers countrywide. The levy is not a
form of taxation. The levy scheme was set up according to the industries and technological
and skill demand in each of them. There are 11 levy orders established to date. Each
industry determines the basis on which it should be levied depending on the nature of the
business. The building and construction industry is lev. A  : 0.25%  of contract value in
excess of Ksh 50,000. Eight industries are charged on per capita basis per employee (i.e.,
Ksh 50 per employee per half year in the commercial and printing industries).
28.    The Industrial Training Act covers the following areas which govern all forms of
industrial training: (1) the DIT; (2) the National Industrial Training Council (a tripartite
body consisting of a Chairman, six representatives of employers (FKE), six representatives of
employees (COTU), and not less than six persons representing other interests); (3) training
committees; (4) training levy orders; (5) training schemes; and (6) trade tests. The Director
of industrial training is the custodian of the Act and secretary to the Council. The DIT is the
secretariat to the National Industrial Training Council.
29.    In 1991 receipts amounted to KSH 61,329,288.20. During that time payments were
made in the amount of Ksh 50,600,460.40. There was a balance of Ksh 154,120,120.35.
The funds are utilized for training in the form of reimbursements to employers for cost of
training and wages paid to employees while in traming. The Director (DI'), acting on advice
of the Council, may make payments out of the Fund for various training purposes, including
payment for courses and travel allowances, grants or loans and reimbursement of an
employer. However, the Levy system is ineffective, due in part to its administration.
Records have been kept manually, the collection and disbursement of funds is cumbersome
and slow. The cost-effectiveness of any training undertaken is not known, nor is its impact on
worker productivity. It is unclear to what extent the levy fund allocations respond to the
industry's requirements and to what extent they are too low or too high.
4/     lTere also exists die Hotel and Caterinug Taining Levy established under the Hotels and Restaurants Act (1971). Tle
Levy is on the customers and not on the hoteliers. The catering industy uses the fiads for training their personnel in the UTALI
College. The Colege offers courses in hotel management (4 years) to local and foreign students. Two-year courses exist in food
production. Enrollment totals about 300 to 400 students. The training under this levy is mainly confined to training manpower
for the high class hotels, it does not cater to middle class hotels and restaurant busiesses. Other caterug training instions are
found, but they are not covered under the levy.



84                                      ANNEX 6
Page 9 of 13
30.     Training schemes which are currently reimbursable fall into the following categories:
(1) management (overseas and local), (2) technician courses, (3) craft courses, (4) indentured
learners S/, and (5) skill improvement courses. Training schemes are drawn up by the
training committees according to training and skills needed in the industry, i.e., local
management and supervisory courses, technician and craft courses in various trades, in-plant
indenured learner, and skldl improvement courses. Most courses related to these schemes are
conducted at various public and private institutions. Management courses are conducted
locally by all national universities and colleges (i.e., KIBT), overseas, and by approved
private management institutions or consultancies.
5/1      An indentred learer is defined as a person bound by a written coract to serve an employer for a determined
period of time (6 monrths to 5 years) for the purpose of leaning a trade or occupation in which he or she is so employed, during
which Ihe employer wfll pay the indetmred learner a wage. Any person who has completed a Certificate of Primary Education
and is 15 years of age or more may apply to become an indentured learner. An employer nmay apply for permission (from the
DMl) to employ several indentured leamners.

REPUBLIC OF KENYA
MICRO- AND SMALL ENTERPRISE TRAINING AND TECHNOLOGY P'0JECT
THE MINISTRY OF RESEARCH, TECHNICAL TRAINING
AND TECHNOLOGY (MRTT&T)
Background
1.    The Ministry of Technical Training and Applied Technology (MTTAT) was established in March
1988, and for the first time, technical and vocational training are the primary responsibility of a single
ministry (as contained in the Presidential Circular No. 1 of 1988). It is also charged with the
development of programs benefitting the informal sector. The creation of the MTTAT and the explicit
inclusion of the Jua Kali or productive informal sector in its portfolio must be recognized as a major step
in increasing the relevance of fonnal training. However, to translate this policy into practice has
inevitably been difficult because of insufficient technical capacity. It is clear that MTTAT had an
inherited responsibility for a wide range of training institutions whose existing purposes and practices
cannot be transformed or rationalized overnight.
2.    In early 1993, the MTTAT was merged with the Ministry of Research, Science and Technology
and was reorganized under the name of Ministry of Research, Technical Training and Technology
(MRTT&T) with an expanded mandate. A draft schedule of duties was provided by the MRTT&T and
is sunmarized below:
3.    Overall fimctions:
National research development policy
National Council of Science and Technology
Liaison with research institutes and other research bodies
Application and transfer of appropriate technology
Research inventory and dissemination
Control of the use of research information
Patents policy
Technic.1 training policy
Technical education
Institutes of Technology
National Polytechnics



90
ANNEX 7
Page 2 of 3
Youth Polytechnics
National Industrial Training Council
Technical Vocational Training
Jua Kali Development Programs
Directorate of Industrial Training
4.    Before the creation of MITAT, YPs were under the charge of the Ministry of Culture and Social
Services. The ITs and NPs came under the Ministry of Education, Science and Technology as well as
the TTIs, which used to be technical high schools. The DIT and technical industrial trdining were the
responsibility of the Ministry of Labor.
MRTT&T Directorates
1)    Technical and Vocational Education/Training (DTT)
2)    Industrial Training - Formal Apprenticeship (Dr)T
3)    Applied Technology - Jha Kali Development Programs (DAT)
4)    Research, Science and Technology Development (DRD)
5)    Kenya Industrial Property Office (KIPO)
6)    National Council for Science and Technology (NCST)
5.    There are six Research Institutes, including KIRDI, with approximately 10,000 employees under
the supervision of the DRD. They are not on the payroll of the MRTT&T, although the budget passes
through MRTT&T.
6.    The total number of employees at the MRTT&T and its provincial services is estimated at 1,202
as of October 1993. This figure is below the approved number of staff by the DMP (about 380 persons).
For instance:

8.    DIT. The DIT is responsible for the operation of the ITCs. It also administers the collection
and allocation of the Industrial Training Levy through the tripartite National Industrial Training Council
(NITC). The levy fund was established in 1971 to ensure emjloyer participation in finanicing training
of industrial manpower (see Annex 5). So far it has been used to support the training of employees of
the levy contributors (i.e., workers or trainees already in industry such as apprentice-, technician-,
indentured learners) as well as employees of non-contributors (i.e., Jua Kali trainees).
9.    The DIT is semi-autonomous and tensions arise from time to time between the DIT, which
operates government facilities and administers the semi-private Industrial Training Fund (parastatal
organizatiors contribute to the Fund), and the other directorates of MRTT&T. A better organization of
MRTT&T and especially an effective management system of the Industrial Training Fund are needed to
strengthen the system's efficiency, effectiveness and access. This would include an effective supervision
and accounting syefem and regular auditing of the accounts, as weil as regular evaluation of the quality
of training programs funded and their impact on labor productivity.
10.   DAT. The DAT is responsible for the formulation and implementation of policies relating to Jua
Kali enterprises in the informal sector. The DAT formulates and administers Jua Kali Development
Programs designed to assist the Jua Kali artisans (funded in part by donors, i.e., the Jua Kali
Demonstration Plant Project (UNDP/UNIDO)). DAT monitors the ativities of the Jua Kali associaions
including registration of Jua Kali artisans.
11.   The DAT has a Training and Gender Unit responsible for informal sector training development
as well as for establishing a sensitization program aimed at bringing young women into technical fields
of study and productive work.
